Ruling on Recitation and Saying Ameen in Prayer
Explaining recitation amount and Ameen pronunciation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the obligatory recitation for imam and munfarid, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the imam saying Ameen.

Core Idea
Imam and munfarid must recite Fatihah and a surah; follower stays silent. Ameen is said with madd; the imam's saying of Ameen is disputed within the Hanafi school.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies two key aspects of salah: the obligatory amount of Quranic recitation and the proper pronunciation of Ameen. It also reveals a subtle difference between Abu Hanifah and his students on whether the imam should say Ameen at all. Understanding these details ensures your prayer conforms to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling for recitation
Note that the phrase 'then he recites' refers to the wajib amount: Fatihah plus an additional surah. This applies to the imam and the one praying alone (munfarid).
The follower (muqtadi) does not recite; he praises and remains silent.
2
Analyze the ruling on Ameen
The word Ameen is a verbal noun meaning 'respond and do'. It should be pronounced with madd (extension) and no stress on the meem. Qasr is allowed but less preferable.
The sources Al-Khulasah and Al-Hidayah confirm this.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the imam saying Ameen
Muhammad ibn al-Hasan narrates from Abu Hanifah that the imam does not say Ameen at all (i.e., does not utter it). However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad themselves held that the imam says it silently.
The text is cut off, but the later Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that the imam says Ameen silently, while the follower says it audibly after the imam.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and its evidence
The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is that the imam says Ameen silently. This is preferred by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah and is the position given in fatwa.
The Shafi'i school differs, holding that the imam says Ameen aloud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقرأ، ويؤمن بعد
قوله : ثم يقرأ ؛ أي مقدار الواجب الذي علم سابقاً من الفاتحة وسورة معها ؛ أي قدرها، هذا إذا كان إماماً أو منفرداً ، وأما المؤتم فيثنى ولا يتعوذ، ولا يسمي، ولا يقرأ على ما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى.
قوله : ويؤمن ؛ مضارع من التأمين ؛ أي يقول المصلّي إماماً كان أو منفرداً : آمين بالمد ومعناه : استجب وافعل، ويجوز القصر أيضاً، والمختار الأول، وتشديد الميم خطأ. كذا في «الخلاصة» و«الهداية».
وحكى محمد في «الموطأ عن أبي حنيفة أن الإمام لا يقول : آمين، بل هو
Then he recites, and after that he says Ameen. His statement: 'Then he recites' means the obligatory amount which was previously known: Fatihah and a surah with it, i.e., its measure. This is if he is an imam or alone. As for the follower, he praises, does not seek refuge, does not say the basmala, and does not recite, as will come, Allah willing. His statement: 'and he says Ameen' is a present tense verb from ta'meen, i.e., the praying person, whether imam or alone, says: 'Ameen' with madd, and its meaning is: 'Respond and do'. Qasr is also permissible, but the first is preferred. Stressing the meem is a mistake. Thus in Al-Khulasah and Al-Hidayah. And Muhammad narrated in Al-Muwatta from Abu Hanifah that the imam does not say Ameen, rather he...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary. It addresses two rulings: first, the amount of Quranic recitation after the takbir in prayer. The commentator clarifies that 'then he recites' refers to the obligatory (wajib) amount: Surah Fatihah plus an additional surah or its equivalent. This applies to the imam and the one praying alone (munfarid). The follower (muqtadi), however, does not recite; he only praises (thana) and does not say ta'awwudh, basmala, or recite Quran. This is the established Hanafi ruling. Second, the ruling on saying Ameen: the word 'Ameen' is said with madd (prolongation) by both the imam and the munfarid. Its meaning is 'respond and do'. Qasr (shortening) is also permissible, but the preferred way is with madd. Stressing the meem (Ameen with shaddah) is a mistake. The commentator cites Al-Khulasah and Al-Hidayah as sources. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is reported: Muhammad ibn al-Hasan narrated in Al-Muwatta that according to Abu Hanifah, the imam does not say Ameen (i.e., aloud or at all? The text is cut off, but the Hanafi position is that the imam says it silently while the follower says it aloud). This shows a difference between Abu Hanifah and later Hanafi scholars who hold that the imam should say Ameen silently.

Key Terms
آمين (Ameen): A word said after reciting Fatihah meaning 'respond and do', used in prayer and supplication.
المؤتم (al-mu'tam): The follower in congregational prayer (muqtadi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the imam saying Ameen based on the narration from Abu Hanifah in Al-Muwatta?
A) The imam says Ameen loudly.
B) The imam does not say Ameen.
C) The imam says Ameen silently.
D) The imam says Ameen with qasr.

Key Takeaway
The imam and munfarid must recite Fatihah and a surah; the follower remains silent. Ameen is said with madd, and the mufta bihi is that the imam says it silently.

Saying Ameen Silently in Prayer
Ruling on saying Ameen silently with proofs from Quran and hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying Ameen in prayer and identify the Quranic verse and Companion practice used as proofs.

Core Idea
Ameen should be said silently because it is a supplication, and the Quran commands secrecy in supplication.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position that Ameen is to be said silently in prayer. The author cites a Quranic verse and reports from the Companions Umar and Ali. Interestingly, a hadith that appears to support silent Ameen is deemed weak by hadith critics, yet the Hanafi school adheres to the silent practice based on stronger evidence. Understanding this ruling reveals how the Hanafi school prioritizes general Quranic principles over specific but weak hadith reports.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
What is the Hanafi ruling on saying Ameen in prayer? Identify that it is sunnah mu'akkada to say it silently, and saying it aloud is makruh tanzihi.
2
Identify the primary proof
Which Quranic verse is cited? Find al-A'raf 7:55 and understand its command to make supplication secretly.
The verse is general, but the Hanafi school applies it to Ameen because Ameen is a supplication.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does the verse apply to Ameen? The term 'supplication' (du'a) includes saying Ameen; therefore, the command for secrecy covers it.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
What hadith is presented and why is it considered weak? The hadith from Wa'il reports the Prophet lowering his voice for Ameen, but hadith critics found a mistake in the chain (from Shu'bah), and the correct version says 'and he said it aloud.'
This shows that a specific hadith, even if seemingly supportive, can be overridden by stronger evidence.
5
Understand the prioritization
Why do Hanafis still prefer silence despite the weak hadith? Because the Quranic principle and the practice of the Khulafa' al-Rashidin (Umar and Ali) provide stronger, more reliable evidence.
Hanafi methodology gives weight to general principles and confirmed companion practice over a hadith with chain issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : سرا؛ هذا هو المأثور عن عمر و علي : «أنهما لم يكونا يجهران ببسم الله ولا بـآمين»، أخرجه الطبري في تهذيب الآثار، كذا ذكره العيني في «عمدة القاري شرح صحيح البخاري». ويؤيده أن آمين دعاء، والأصل في الدعاء الإخفاء؛ لقوله تعالى: ادْعُوا رَبَّكُمْ تَضَرُّعًا وَخُفْيَةً، وأخرج أحمد وأبو داود والترمذي والطبراني والدارقطني والحاكم وغيرهم عن وائل: «إنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لَمَّا بلغ غير المغضوب عليهم ولا الضالين قال: آمين، وخفض بها صوته». وفي رواية: «وأخفى بها»، لكن اتفق الحفاظ، وإليهم المرجع في تنقيد الأسانيد أنّ في سندِهِ خدشة، وخطأ من شعبة أحد رواته، والصحيح «فجهر بها».
His saying: 'silently' – this is what is reported from Umar and Ali: 'They would not recite Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim nor Ameen aloud,' narrated by al-Tabari in Tahdhib al-Athar, as mentioned by al-Ayni in Umdat al-Qari. And it is supported by the fact that Ameen is a supplication, and the principle for supplication is concealment, due to His saying: 'Call upon your Lord humbly and secretly' (al-A'raf 55). And Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, al-Hakim and others narrated from Wa'il: 'When the Prophet reached 'Not those who have earned Your anger nor those who have gone astray', he said Ameen and lowered his voice with it.' And in one narration: 'And he concealed it,' but the memorizers of hadith, who are the reference for scrutinizing chains, agreed that its chain has a flaw – a mistake from Shu'bah, one of its narrators – and the correct (narration) is 'and he said it aloud'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on saying Ameen audibly (jahr) versus silently (sirr) after Fatiha. The author states that the relied-upon practice is to say it silently, as reported from the caliphs Umar and Ali. The evidence is twofold: (1) Ameen is a supplication, and the Quran commands that supplication be made humbly and secretly; (2) A hadith from Wa'il reports that the Prophet lowered his voice when saying Ameen. However, the author notes that hadith critics have declared this narration weak due to a mistake in the chain, and the correct version says the Prophet spoke it aloud. Despite this, the author still presents the silent practice as established from the Companions. This reflects the Hanafi preference for silence in Ameen, based on the act of the righteous predecessors and the general principle of concealing supplication.

Key Terms
سراً (sirran): Silently, in a low voice that is not audible to others
آمين (Ameen): A word said after Fatiha meaning 'O Allah, respond (to our supplication)'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is used to support saying Ameen silently?
A) Al-Fatiha 1:1
B) Al-A'raf 7:55
C) Al-Baqarah 2:185
D) Al-Ikhlas 112:1

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling to say Ameen silently is based on the Quranic principle of hiding supplication and the practice of the early Companions, despite a weak narration that suggests otherwise.

Ruling on Saying Ameen Silently
Expounds the ruling of saying Ameen silently with proofs and a dispute over jahr vs sirr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying Ameen silently in prayer and explain the primary evidence and the basis of the dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Ameen is a supplication, and the origin in supplication is concealment.

This passage from the matn 'al-Wiqayah' instructs that the imam and follower say 'Ameen' silently after reciting Surah al-Fatihah. The author expands on the proofs, addressing the well-known dispute with the Shafi'i school over whether Ameen should be said aloud or silently. We will walk through the Hanafi evidence and the response to the opposing view, clarifying why silence is the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its applicability
The matn states 'nor of those who go astray, silently'. This ruling is specific to the one praying alone (munfarid) and the follower (muqtadi).
The imam's ruling is not explicitly mentioned here; other texts clarify imam also says it silently.
2
Analyze the primary hadith proof
The author cites the hadith: 'When the Imam says… then say Ameen.' He argues this specifies the time for the follower's Ameen, not a shared utterance (which would negate the division between imam and follower).
The hadith does not mention loudness; the Hanafi school infers silence from other indications.
3
Examine the evidence for silence (sirr)
The author presents: (1) the practice of Umar and Ali - they did not say Ameen aloud; (2) the Quranic verse urging secret supplication; (3) a hadith from Wail that the Prophet lowered his voice when saying Ameen. However, he notes the latter is weak.
The weak hadith is used as supporting evidence, not primary.
4
Address the opposing Shafi'i evidence
The author acknowledges that the hadith masters authenticate the version where the Prophet said Ameen aloud. Multiple strong chains support jahr. Some later Hanafis like Ibn al-Humam admitted the strength of jahr narrations.
Despite this, the school holds to sirr based on the practice of the Rightly Guided Caliphs and the principle of du'a.
5
Understand the tarjih and the final fatwa
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that Ameen is said silently. This is based on the matn, the practice of the caliphs, and the principle that supplication is best done secretly. The dispute is considered legitimate (ikhtilaf) and should not cause hostility.
The passage warns against the sectarian rejection of the other view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا الضالين سراً) مختص بالمنفرد والمأموم، وسنده فيه حديث : إذا قال الإمام : عَلَيْهِمْ غَيْرِ الْمَغْضُوبِ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا الضالين ، فقولوا آمين ) ، أخرجه البخاري والترمذي ومسلم وأبو داود وغيرهم، فإنّ هذه قسمة، والقسمة تنافي الشركة. وحجة جمهور أصحابنا وغيرهم حديث: «إذا أمن الإمام فأمنول) ، أخرجه الأئمة السنة. وليس المقصود من الأوّل بيان القسمة، بل بيان وقتِ تأمين المقتدي ، والإشارة إلى معية تأمين الإمام والمقتدي. [١]قوله : سرا ؛ هذا هو المأثور عن عمر و علي : «أنهما لم يكونا يجهران ببسم الله ولا بـآمين (۳) ، أخرجه الطبري في تهذيب الآثار، كذا ذكره العيني في «عمدة القاري شرح صحيح البخاري). ويؤيده أن آمين دعاء، والأصل في الدعاء الإخفاء ؛ لقوله ولا : ادْعُوا رَبَّكُمْ تَضَرُّعًا وَخُفْيَةً ) ، وأخرج أحمد وأبو داود والترمذي والطبراني والدارقطني والحاكم وغيرهم عن وائل : «إنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لَمَّا بلغ غير المغضوب عليهم ولا الضالين قال : آمين، وخفض بها صوته وفي رواية: «وأخفى بها ، لكن اتفق الحفاظ، وإليهم المرجع في تنقيد الأسانيد أنّ في سندِهِ خدشة، وخطأ من شعبة أحد رواته، والصحيح «فجهر بها». وقد ثبت الجهر عن رسول الله له بأسانيد متعدّدة يقوي بعضها بعضا(۱) في «سنن ابن ماجة» والنسائي وأبي داود، و «جامع الترمذي»، و«صحيح ابن حبان»، وكتاب «الأم» للشافعي، وغيرها، وعن جمع من أصحابه بــروايـات ابـن حـبان في «كتاب الثقات» وغيره. ولهذا أشار بعض أصحابنا كابن الهمام في «فتح القدير»، وتلميذه ابنُ أمير حاج في «حلبة المجلي شرح مُنية المصلي» إلى قوته رواية، كما بسطته في «التعليق الممجد على موطأ محمد» وفي «السعاية». ولقد طال النزاع في هذه المسألة وما يماثلها في عصرنا بين علماء عصرنا وعوام دهرنا، فأنكر كلّ من الطائفتين ورد ما ادعته الأخرى مطلقاً، فضلوا وأضلوا، عصمنا الله منه.
(And not those who go astray, silently) This is specific to the one praying alone and the follower. Its sanad includes the hadith: When the Imam says '...not those with whom Allah is angry, and not those who go astray', then say Ameen. Narrated by Bukhari, Tirmidhi, Muslim, Abu Dawud and others. For this is a division, and division negates partnership. The proof of the majority of our companions and others is the hadith: 'When the Imam says Ameen, then say Ameen.' Narrated by the six Imams. The objective of the first is not to explain the division, but to explain the time of the follower's Ameen, and to indicate the concomitance of the Ameen of the Imam and the follower. [1] His saying: silently; this is what is reported from Umar and Ali, that they did not say Bismillah aloud nor Ameen. Narrated by Tabari in Tahdhib al-Athar, as mentioned by Ayni in Umdat al-Qari. It is supported by the fact that Ameen is a supplication, and the origin in supplication is concealment, due to His saying: 'Call upon your Lord in humility and privately.' Ahmad, Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Tabarani, Darqutni, Hakim and others narrated from Wail: 'When the Prophet reached 'not those with whom Allah is angry and not those who go astray', he said Ameen and lowered his voice with it.' In another narration: 'And he concealed it.' But the majority of hadith masters, to whom reference is made in scrutinizing chains, agreed that there is a defect in its chain, a mistake from Shu'ba one of its narrators, and the correct is 'and he said it aloud.' And it is authenticated. The public recitation aloud has been established from the Prophet with multiple chains that strengthen each other, in Sunan Ibn Majah, Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Jami' al-Tirmidhi, Sahih Ibn Hibban, and the book al-Umm of al-Shafi'i, and others, and from a group of his Companions through narrations of Ibn Hibban in Kitab al-Thiqat and others. For this reason, some of our companions, such as Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir, and his student Ibn Amir Haj in Halbat al-Mujalli, pointed to its strength in narration, as I elaborated in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad and al-Si'aya. And indeed the dispute has long continued in this issue and its like in our time between the scholars of our time and the commoners of our age, each of the two groups rejecting and denying what the other claimed absolutely, so they went astray and led astray. May Allah protect us from that.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the manner of saying Ameen after Surah al-Fatihah in prayer. The matn states that the imam and follower say 'Ameen' silently (sirran). The author begins by explaining that this ruling applies specifically to the one praying alone or the follower, not the imam? Actually, the text says it is specific to the individual and the follower. Then he provides the proof: the hadith reported by Bukhari and others where the Prophet instructed the companions to say Ameen when the imam reaches 'ghayril maghdubi 'alayhim wa la d-dallin'. The author clarifies that the purpose of this hadith is not to indicate that the imam and follower share the Ameen (which would negate the division of roles), but to specify the time when the follower says Ameen, and to indicate that the follower's Ameen is simultaneous with the imam's. Then the author addresses the word 'sirran' (silently). He cites traditions from Umar and Ali that they did not say Bismillah or Ameen aloud, and supports this with the Quranic verse about supplicating secretly. He then presents the counter-narrations: a hadith from Wail ibn Hujr that the Prophet said Ameen audibly. However, the hadith masters (huffaz) have pointed out a defect in its chain, attributing the error to Shu'ba. The correct version is that he said it aloud. The author notes that numerous chains strengthen the jahr (audible) version, found in major hadith collections. He mentions that some later Hanafi scholars like Ibn al-Humam acknowledged the strength of the jahr narration in terms of hadith criticism, but the school ruling (mufta bihi) remains sirr. The passage concludes with a lament about the ongoing dispute among scholars and commoners, each faction stubbornly rejecting the other. The commentary emphasizes that the relied-upon Hanafi position is sirr, but the matter is not free from scholarly debate.

Key Terms
آمين (Ameen): A word said after reciting Surah al-Fatihah, meaning 'O Allah, respond' or 'so be it'. It is a supplication.
سراً (sirran): Silently, with a low voice that cannot be heard by others.
المقتدي (al-muqtadi): The follower in congregational prayer, the one who prays behind the imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is Ameen said by the follower in congregational prayer?
A) Silently (sirr)
B) Aloud (jahr)
C) Either, whichever the imam does
D) Only if the imam says it aloud

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that Ameen is said silently in prayer, based on the practice of the Caliphs and the principle that supplication is best concealed, despite stronger hadith evidence for saying it aloud.

Timing and Manner of Takbeer for Ruku
Commentary on the timing of takbeer for ruku

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three implications of the rule that the takbeer for ruku occurs after completing recitation, and explain the Hanafi ruling on its wajib status.

Core Idea
The takbeer for ruku is a wajib transition that must follow complete recitation, not overlap with it.

The takbeer for ruku is a key transition in prayer, moving from standing (qiyam) to bowing (ruku'). The classical Hanafi texts clearly state that this takbeer must occur after finishing the recitation. This seemingly simple rule carries three important implications that shape the proper order of prayer. In this lesson, we will unpack those implications and examine the ruling's wajib status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act being ruled upon is the takbeer (saying 'Allahu Akbar') for the transition to ruku. According to the Hanafi school, this takbeer is wajib (obligatory) — not fard, meaning its omission does not invalidate the prayer but requires sajdah al-sahw.
The wajib level is based on the strength of the dalil; here, the evidence is from the practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) which is zanni al-thubut.
2
Understand the three implications from the commentary
The commentator draws three implications from the phrase 'thumma yukabbiru lil ruku': (i) the time for ruku begins after finishing recitation; (ii) one should not recite any Quran after the takbeer for ruku; (iii) the recitation should not be directly joined to the takbeer — there should be a natural pause or separation.
This ensures that each action in prayer is performed in its proper place, avoiding confusion between the acts.
3
Note the follower's comparison (tashbih)
The commentator compares the follower (ma'mum) in the takbeer to the follower in saying Ameen — both should be done silently. This reinforces that the follower should not recite audibly after the imam's takbeer.
The comparison is about the mode (silent) and not about the ruling itself, as the takbeer is wajib while Ameen is sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كالمؤتم ؛ أي المأموم كما في نسخة، وهو المقتدي ، وهذا التشبيه في نفس التأمين أو في الإسرار به أو فيهما ، وإنّما شبهه به ؛ لأنَّ تأمين المأموم لا خلاف فيه ، وإسراره أيضاً أظهر ؛ لأن المأموم مأمور بالإنصات في جميع الأذكار. (٢) قوله : ثم يكبر فيه إشارة إلى أنّ وقت الركوع بعد الفراغ من القراءة، وإلى أن لا يقرأ شيئاً بعد تكبير الركوع، وإلى أن لا يصل القراءة بالتكبير ، وهذا التكبير
His saying: 'like the follower' means the one being led, as in the copy, i.e., the muqtadi. This comparison is either in the act of Ameen itself, or in saying it silently, or in both. He only compared him because the follower's Ameen is not disputed, and its silence is also more apparent because the follower is commanded to listen attentively in all remembrances. (2) His saying: 'then he says the takbeer for ruku' indicates that the time for ruku is after finishing the recitation, and that one should not recite anything after the takbeer for ruku, and that one should not join the recitation with the takbeer. And this takbeer...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the takbeer for ruku. The matn 'thumma yukabbiru lil ruku'' is a directive that after completing the recitation of the Quran in prayer, one should say the takbeer and move into ruku. The commentator explains that this rule implies three things: first, that the time for ruku begins after finishing the recitation; second, that one should not recite any additional Quran after the takbeer for ruku; and third, that one should not connect the recitation directly to the takbeer (i.e., there should be a pause or separation). The commentator also mentions a comparison regarding the follower: just as the follower says Ameen silently, similarly the follower's takbeer is done quietly. However, the text is cut off and does not complete the discussion. The ruling is straightforward: the takbeer for ruku must be performed after recitation, and it marks the transition from qiyam to ruku.

Key Terms
تكبير (takbeer): Saying 'Allahu Akbar' to transition between prayer positions. Specifically, the takbeer for ruku transitions from standing to bowing.
الركوع (ruku'): Bowing in prayer, a pillar (rukn) of salat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Before starting the recitation
B) After finishing the recitation
C) While reciting, to show the end
D) It can be said at any time during the standing

Key Takeaway
The takbeer for ruku is a wajib act that must be performed after completing recitation, without joining the two, and with no recitation after it; its omission requires sajdah al-sahw.

Ruling on Imam Saying Amin and Takbir for Bowing
Commentarial expansion of a matn rule on imam's Amin and Takbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether the imam says Amin aloud or silently and derive three practical indications from the phrase 'then he says Takbir for bowing.'

Core Idea
The imam follows the follower in saying Amin silently; the Takbir for bowing begins after recitation without connecting them.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains two actions of the imam in prayer: his saying of Amin and his Takbir for bowing. The matn states simply 'like the follower, then he says Takbir for bowing.' The commentator expands on both parts, clarifying the standard Hanafi position that the imam says Amin silently, and then discusses the disputed narrations about saying it aloud. He also draws three practical indications from the wording 'then he says Takbir.' Understanding these rulings is essential for imams and followers in congregational prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The matn covers two distinct actions: the imam's saying of Amin (after al-Fatihah) and his Takbir for bowing. The ruling on each is derived from the phrasing.
Note the connector 'then' (ثم) which indicates sequence.
2
Analyze the first ruling: the imam is like the follower
The commentator explains that the similarity is in the saying of Amin itself, in doing it silently, or both. He emphasizes that the follower's Amin is silent because he is commanded to listen, so the imam should also be silent.
This is the standard Hanafi position; opposing views cite hadiths for jahr.
3
Examine the dispute about jahr (loud Amin)
The commentator notes that narrations supporting loud Amin exist in major hadith collections and some later Hanafis (e.g., Ibn al-Humam) considered them strong. However, the matn and the majority uphold silent Amin as the relied-upon position.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf; the practical consequence is whether the imam's Amin is audible.
4
Analyze the second ruling: timing of Takbir
From 'then he says Takbir for bowing,' the commentator derives three indications: (1) the time for bowing is after finishing recitation, (2) nothing should be recited after the Takbir, and (3) the recitation should not be directly connected to the Takbir (a pause is recommended).
These are procedural rules for the imam.

Commentary
This is a commentary passage explaining a matn that states the imam says Amin like the follower and then says Takbir for bowing. The commentator first notes that the loud pronunciation of 'Amin' has been established from the Prophet through many chains, citing several hadith collections. Some Hanafi scholars like Ibn al-Humam considered the narration strong. The commentator then remarks on the modern dispute on this matter. Sunnah Actions of Bowing (Rukūʿ) in Prayer
Describes the sunnah actions of bowing: lowering, hand placement, back straightness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah actions of rukūʿ according to the Hanafi school and explain the preferred timing of the Takbir.

Core Idea
The sunnah of rukūʿ: Takbir while lowering, hands on knees, back straight, head aligned.

The bowing posture (rukūʿ) is a pillar of prayer, but its precise performance is guided by the Prophet's sunnah. This lesson unpacks the classical commentary on the actions that make rukūʿ complete: from the timing of the Takbir to the placement of hands and the straightening of the back. Understanding these details elevates the quality of your salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions described
The passage lists four actions: lowering (khafḍ) while saying Takbir, leaning on the knees with spread fingers, extending the back straight, and not raising the head. List them clearly.
2
Examine the evidence for each action
For each action, note the hadith cited: Takbir with movement, hand placement on knees from Mus'ab ibn Sa'd, back straightness from Ibn Majah. Understand the proof structure.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on Takbir timing
The commentary notes a difference: some hold Takbir before bowing, but the correct view is simultaneous with lowering. Identify the preferred view and its source (al-Nahr al-Fa'iq, al-Munya).
4
Apply the description to your own prayer
Visualize and practice: as you say Allāhu Akbar, begin lowering; place palms firmly on knees with fingers spread; ensure your back is level; keep your head in line with your spine. This is the sunnah.

Lowering and leaning with his hands on his knees, spreading his fingers, extending his back, not raising (the head). The transitions are an established sunnah with many hadiths, which we have mentioned in al-Si'ayah, including the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud: 'The Messenger of Allah used to say Takbir at every raising and lowering, standing and sitting,' reported by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ahmad and others. [2] His saying: 'And he leans' means he takes the knees with his palms and leans on them, because of what the six leading imams narrated from Mus'ab ibn Sa'd that he said: 'I prayed next to my father and I clasped my hands and placed them between my thighs, but my father forbade me and said: We used to do it but we were forbidden from it, and we were commanded to place our hands on the knees.' [3] His saying: 'Extending his back' — with a fatha on the za', in Persian 'yusht' — means he makes his back straight in bowing so that if a cup of water were poured on it, it would stay still. Commentary
This passage describes the sunnah actions of the rukūʿ posture. It is a commentary on the matn that lists the actions: lowering while saying Takbir, placing hands on knees with spread fingers, straightening the back, and not raising the head. The commentator explains each term. 'Lowering' is a state of the person saying the Takbir, meaning the Takbir should be performed during the lowering motion, not before or after; this is the preferred view. 'He leans' means he places his palms on his knees and leans on them, supported by a hadith from Mus'ab ibn Sa'd where the father forbade clasping hands and instead instructed placing hands on knees. 'Extending his back' means making the back flat and level, so that water would not spill. This is from the practice of the Prophet. 'Not raising' his head means keeping the head aligned with the back, not lifting it.

خافضاً (khafidan): Lowering; the act of bending down for rukūʿ
باسطاً ظهره (basitan zahrahu): Extending the back; making it straight and level in rukūʿ

The Hanafi school teaches that the Takbir for rukūʿ accompanies the lowering motion, hands rest on knees with spread fingers, the back is level, and the head is not raised—all sunnah muakkadah.

Imam's Tasmi' and Tahmid: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on imam's tahmid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether the imam says tahmid after tasmi', identify the evidence for each, and explain which view is the mufta bihi and why.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifah said imam says only tasmi'; later Hanafis say imam adds tahmid silently — the latter is relied upon.

This passage addresses a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute: should the imam say 'Rabbana lakal hamd' after 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' when rising from ruku'? The author presents the positions of Abu Hanifah, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with the later tarjih. Understanding this dispute will sharpen your ability to weigh conflicting hadith evidence and see how the Hanafi school evolves through its later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
The contested point is whether the imam says tahmid after tasmi' when rising from ruku'. State: what does Abu Hanifah hold? What do Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and the later majority hold?
2
Examine the evidence for each position
Abu Hanifah relies on the division hadith: 'When the imam says Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, then say Rabbana lakal hamd.' The opposing view uses multiple hadiths where the Prophet ﷺ said both. Identify the type of dalil (sunnah) and its epistemological weight.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing evidence
The later Hanafis argue that the division hadith does not preclude the imam from also saying tahmid silently; rather, it assigns the follower's response. They hold that authentic practice of the Prophet ﷺ overrides. How is the division hadith interpreted or its applicability limited?
Note the concept of 'abrogation' or 'specification' mentioned in the commentary.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The passage states that most later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) consider the view that the imam says both tasmi' and tahmid silently as al-ashah (most correct). Confirm the authority_weight (fatawa mutakhkhirin) and why it is preferred.
5
Apply to practice: follower and munfarid
The passage also mentions that the follower says only tahmid, and the munfarid (person praying alone) says both tasmi' and tahmid. Verify these rulings from the text.

[2] His saying: 'which is the minimum'; meaning tathlith (three times) is the minimum, and it is better to increase beyond that, saying seven or nine or more, and if he decreases from three he abandons the sunnah, and likewise in prostration; due to the hadith: 'When one of you bows, let him say three times: Subhana Rabbiyal-'Azeem, and that is the minimum, and when he prostrates, let him say: Subhana Rabbiyal-A'la three times, and that is the minimum,' reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. And the command therein is for recommendation not obligation by consensus of those considered reliable, as we have elaborated in al-Si'ayah. This is concerning ruku'. [3] His saying: 'raising his head'; there is an indication of the recommendation of synchronizing the tasmi' and lifting from ruku'. [4] His saying: 'and he suffices with it'; i.e., with the tasmi', meaning the imam does not say 'Rabbana lakal hamd'; this is the well-known position of Abu Hanifah, taken from the hadith: 'When the imam says Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, then say: Rabbana lakal hamd,' for this is a division, and division precludes partnership, and it is the standard according to the six imams and others. And the position adopted by the majority and Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and it is reported from Abu Hanifah that the imam also says: 'Rabbana lakal hamd' silently after the tasmi', and it was chosen by al-Fadli, al-Tahawi, al-Shurunbulali, the author of al-Munyah, and most of the later scholars among our companions. And it is the most correct, agreeing with what is established that he (the Prophet) used to say after Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah: 'Rabbana lakal hamd', and in one narration: 'Rabbana wa lakal hamd', and in one narration: 'Allahumma Rabbana lakal hamd', and in one narration: 'Allahumma Rabbana wa lakal hamd', and all this is in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim and other reliable books, and the elaboration is in al-Si'ayah. [1] His saying: 'and with tahmid'; i.e., the follower suffices with tahmid, and the best of its established four wordings is the longest and the minimum is the shortest. [2] His saying: 'and the one praying alone'; i.e., one who is not an imam nor a follower performs tasmi' and then tahmid afterwards, and this is the most correct chosen by the author of al-Hidayah and others, while the author of al-Kanz chose sufficing with tahmid alone.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether the imam should say the tahmid ('Rabbana lakal hamd') after the tasmi' ('Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah') when rising from ruku'. The author begins by stating the well-known position of Abu Hanifah: the imam suffices with tasmi' alone and does not add tahmid. This is based on the hadith which divides the speech between imam and follower: the imam says tasmi' and the follower says tahmid. The division indicates that each has a specific part, so the imam should not combine both. However, the majority of scholars, including the two senior disciples Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and many later Hanafi scholars such as al-Fadli, al-Tahawi, al-Shurunbulali, and the author of al-Munyah, hold that the imam should also say 'Rabbana lakal hamd' silently after the tasmi'. They consider this the most correct position (al-ashah), supported by numerous authentic hadiths where the Prophet ﷺ would say both. The author notes that this is the choice of the later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) and that it agrees with established practice from the Prophet. The passage also covers other related rulings: the proper posture in ruku' (not lowering or raising the head excessively), the minimum tasbih in ruku' and sujud being three times with the sunnah being to increase, and the actions of the follower and the one praying alone regarding tasmi' and tahmid.

Key Terms
تسميع (tasmi'): Saying 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' when rising from ruku'.
تحميد (tahmid): Saying 'Rabbana lakal hamd' or similar praise after tasmi'.
منفرد (munfarid): One who prays alone, not as imam or follower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of this passage, what is the most correct (al-ashah) position regarding the imam's recitation after rising from ruku'?
A) The imam says only tasmi' and no tahmid.
B) The imam says tasmi' aloud and tahmid aloud.
C) The imam says tasmi' silently and then adds 'Rabbana lakal hamd' silently.
D) The imam says nothing; both tasmi' and tahmid are for the follower.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi mufta bihi is that the imam says tasmi' and then adds tahmid silently, harmonizing the division hadith with the Prophet's practice.

Tasmi' and Tahmid for Follower and Individual
Ruling on follower's and individual's tashmi' and tahmid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what the follower (mu'tam) and individual (munfarid) say when rising from ruku', and identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) among the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
The follower says only 'Rabbana lakal hamd'; the individual says both; the imam says tasmi' and then tahmid silently.

When you rise from ruku' in prayer, you may say 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' (tasmi') and 'Rabbana lakal hamd' (tahmid). But who says which? This lesson examines the Hanafi rules for the follower, the individual, and the imam, based on a classical commentary that also discusses an intra-madhhab disagreement. By understanding this, you will correctly apply the sunnah in your prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three roles and two phrases
Recognize that the prayer involves three types of worshippers: imam, follower (mu'tam), and individual (munfarid). The two phrases are tasmi' ('Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah') and tahmid ('Rabbana lakal hamd').
2
State the base matn ruling
The base text (matn) says: the follower suffices with tahmid only, and the individual combines both. The imam's role is not stated in the matn, leading to the dispute.
3
Explore the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Three views exist: (1) The majority (including Sahibayn) and later scholars say the imam says tahmid silently after tasmi'. (2) Some (like author of al-Kanz) say the imam only says tasmi'. (3) A third view says the imam says only tasmi' but no tahmid. Trace each view's authority.
Note that Abu Hanifa is reported to have allowed the imam to say tahmid silently, aligning with the majority.
4
Examine the dalil
The commentator cites authentic hadith from Bukhari and Muslim showing the Prophet said 'Rabbana lakal hamd' after 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah'. This evidence supports the majority view.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text declares the view that the imam says tahmid silently as 'al-asahh' (most correct), and this is adopted by later scholars (al-Hidayah, etc.). Hence the relied-upon ruling: follower: only tahmid; individual: both; imam: tasmi' followed by silent tahmid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالتحميد المؤتم، والمنفرد يجمع بينهما
مخرج ع عند الأئمة الستة وغيرهم.
والذي ذهب إليه الجمهور وأبو يوسف ومحمد الله، وروى عن أبي حنيفة له أنّ الإمام أيضاً يقول: ربنا لك الحمد سراً بعد التسميع، واختاره الفضلي والطحاوي والشرنبلالي، وصاحب «المنية»، وعامة المتأخرين من أصحابنا الله.
وهو الأصح الموافق لما ثبت عنه أنه كان يقول بعد سمع الله لمن حمده : «ربنا لك الحمد»، وفي رواية: «ربنا ولك الحمد»، وفي رواية: «اللهم ربنا لك الحمد»، وفي رواية: «اللهم ربَّنا ولك الحمد»، وذلك كله في صحيح البخاري» و«مسلم» وغيرهما من الكتب المعتبرة، والبسط في «السعاية».
[١]قوله : وبالتحميد ؛ أي يكتفي بالتحميد المقتدى، وأفضل ألفاظه الأربعة الثابتة أطولها وأدناها أخصرها.
[٢]قوله : والمنفرد ؛ أي من ليس بإمام ولا مؤتم يأتي بالتسميع وبالتحميد بعده، وهذا هو الأصح الذي اختاره صاحب الهداية»، وغيره، واختار صاحب (الكنز) الاكتفاء بالتحميد.
والقول الثالث : أنه يأتي بالتسميع لا غير، وصححه في «السراج» معزياً إلى شيخ الإسلام.
ينظر: «درر الحکام (۱) : (۷۱) ورد المحتار» (١ : ٣٣٤).
And by the tahmid the follower [suffices], and the individual combines between both [tashmi' and tahmid]. The hadith is reported by the six imams and others. The position taken by the majority and Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with them) — and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa — is that the imam also says: 'Rabbana lakal hamd' silently after the tashmi', and it was chosen by al-Fadli, al-Tahawi, al-Shurnbulali, the author of al-Munyah, and the generality of later scholars among our companions (may Allah be pleased with them). And it is the most correct (asahh), in agreement with what is established from him (the Prophet) that he used to say after 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah': 'Rabbana lakal hamd', and in a narration: 'Rabbana wa lakal hamd', and in a narration: 'Allahumma Rabbana lakal hamd', and in a narration: 'Allahumma Rabbana wa lakal hamd', and all of that is in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim and other reliable books, and the elaboration is in al-Si'ayah. [1] His statement: 'And by the tahmid' means he contents himself with the tahmid of the one followed, and the best of its four established wordings are the longest, and the least is the shortest. [2] His statement: 'And the individual' means one who is neither imam nor follower comes with the tashmi' and then the tahmid, and this is the most correct (asahh) chosen by the author of al-Hidayah and others, while the author of al-Kanz chose to suffice with the tahmid. And the third view: that he comes with the tashmi' only, and it was deemed correct in al-Siraj attributed to Shaykh al-Islam. Refer: Durar al-Hukkam (1:71) and Radd al-Muhtar (1:334).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the tasmi' (saying 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah') and tahmid (saying 'Rabbana lakal hamd') in prayer for the follower (mu'tam) and the individual (munfarid). The base text (matn) states that the follower suffices with tahmid only, while the individual combines both. The commentator expands, noting the hadith evidence from the Six Books. The majority view (including Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds that the imam also says 'Rabbana lakal hamd' silently after the tashmi', and this is considered the most correct (asahh) by later Hanafi scholars. However, Abu Hanifa himself reportedly allowed it, and some like the author of al-Kanz ruled that the follower should only do tahmid. A third view restricts to tashmi' only. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the follower says only tahmid, the individual says both, and the imam says tahmid silently after tashmi'. This is based on authentic hadith reports of the Prophet's practice.

Key Terms
المؤتم (al-mu'tam): The follower in prayer, the one praying behind an imam.
المنفرد (al-munfarid): The one praying alone, not as imam or follower.
التسميع (al-tasmi'): Saying 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' when rising from ruku'.
التحميد (al-tahmid): Saying 'Rabbana lakal hamd' after rising from ruku'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah'
C) Only 'Rabbana lakal hamd'
D) Nothing, it is silent

Key Takeaway
The follower says only 'Rabbana lakal hamd'; the individual says both; the imam says tasmi' then tahmid silently.

The Sunnah Manner of Prostration: Placing Knees Before Hands
Description of the sunna way to perform sujud in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah sequence of placing limbs in sujud according to the Hanafi school and identify the evidence from the hadith of Kulayb.

Core Idea
The Prophet placed his knees before his hands in sujud; this sunnah sequence is established by authentic hadith.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the recommended manner of prostration in prayer. While the rukn of sujud is placing the forehead on the ground, the Hanafi school emphasizes the sunnah sequence of lowering the limbs: knees first, then hands, then face. This order is based on a specific hadith, and the commentary clarifies related issues like the timing of takbir and the flexibility in hand placement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah acts described
The text specifies standing straight, saying takbir while lowering, placing knees first, then hands, then face between palms, and keeping hands level with ears (or shoulders).
These are all sunnah acts, not fard or wajib.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The hadith of Kulayb explicitly states that the Prophet placed his knees before his hands when prostrating. This is reported with a strong chain by the Sunan compilers.
The hadith is used as the proof for the order.
3
Understand the legal effect of omission
Omitting standing straight requires sajdah al-sahw according to al-Qunyah, indicating it is considered a wajib or at least a sunnah whose omission necessitates compensation. Omitting other sunan like the order does not require compensation.
The consequence varies per act.
4
Note the scope of flexibility
While most narrations place hands level with the ears, a narration from Abu Humayd places them level with the shoulders. The commentator states there is scope (wasi') in this matter.
Both positions are established, so one may choose.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that hands should be placed before knees. The Hanafi school follows the hadith of Kulayb which gives knees precedence.
The difference is based on different hadith or methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقوم مستويا. ثُمَّ يُكَبِّرُ ويسجد، فيضع ركبتيه أولاً، ثُمَّ يديه، ثُمّ وجَهَهُ بين كفيه، ويديه حذاء أذنيه
[1]قوله : ويقوم مستوياً؛ أي بحيث يستقر كل عضو مكانه، فلو تركه وجب سجود السهو كذا في «القنية».
[٢] قوله : ويسجد؛ لم يقل : ساجداً؛ ليفيد مقارنة التكبير مع السجود، وتنبيهاً على أن ابتداء التكبير عند ابتداء الانخفاض، وانتهاءه عند وضع جبهته للسجود، صرح به في «المحيط».
[٣] قوله : فيضع؛ تفسير للسجود، وبيان لكيفيته الثابتة بحديث كليب له، قال : «رأیت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا سجد يضع ركبتيه قبل يديه، وإذا نهض رفع يديه قبل ركبتيه»، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة وأحمد والدارمي وغيرهم بسند قوي.
[٤]قوله : بين كفّيه؛ كذا روى من فعل الرسول الله، أخرجه الطحاوي.
[٥] قوله : حذاء أذنيه؛ بكسر الحاء؛ أي مقابل أذنيه، كذا ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برواية وائل، أخرجه مسلم والنسائي والطحاوي، وثبت عنها أيضاً برواية أبي حميد الساعدي عند أبي داود والترمذي وضع اليدين حذو المنكبين، والأمر فيه واسع.
And stand straight. Then he says takbir and prostrates, and he places his knees first, then his hands, then his face between his palms, and his hands parallel to his ears. [1] His statement: 'And stand straight' means such that every limb settles in its place. If he omits it, sajdah al-sahw becomes obligatory. Thus in al-Qunyah. [2] His statement: 'and prostrates' — he did not say 'prostrating' to indicate the simultaneity of the takbir with the prostration, and as a hint that the start of the takbir is at the start of lowering, and its end at placing the forehead for prostration. This is stated explicitly in al-Muhit. [3] His statement: 'he places' is an interpretation of prostration and an explanation of its manner established by the hadith of Kulayb, who said: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah, when he prostrated, place his knees before his hands, and when he rose, he raised his hands before his knees.' It was reported by the authors of the four Sunans, Ahmad, al-Darimi, and others with a strong chain. [4] His statement: 'between his palms' — thus it is narrated from the action of the Messenger of Allah, reported by al-Tahawi. [5] His statement: 'parallel to his ears' — with a kasrah on the ha, i.e., opposite his ears. Thus it is established from the Messenger of Allah in the narration of Wa'il, reported by Muslim, al-Nasa'i, and al-Tahawi. And also established from him in the narration of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi, reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi, placing the hands parallel to the shoulders, and there is scope in this matter.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage details the sunna manner of prostration (sujud) in prayer. The base text instructs: stand straight, then say takbir and prostrate, placing knees first, then hands, then face between the palms, with hands level with the ears. The commentary expands on each element. First, standing straight is necessary; if one intentionally omits it, sajdah al-sahw becomes wajib. The connection between takbir and the beginning of the lowering movement is important: the takbir starts as one begins to lower into sujud and ends upon placing the forehead. The order of placing limbs is established by the hadith of Kulayb, which is reported with a strong chain: the Prophet would place his knees before his hands when going down, and when rising, he would raise his hands before his knees. This is the sunna sequence. The face is placed between the palms (not on the palms, but between them). As for the hands, most narrations say they are placed level with the ears, but some narrations indicate level with the shoulders; there is flexibility (al-amru fihi wasi'). The commentator notes that both positions are established, and one may choose.

Key Terms
سجود السهو (sajdah al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness performed to compensate for an omission of a wajib or sunnah in prayer.
حذاء أذنيه (hudhaa udhunayhi): Parallel to the ears; the recommended position of the hands during prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which hadith is cited as evidence for placing knees before hands in sujud?
A) Hadith of Ibn Mas'ud
B) Hadith of Kulayb
C) Hadith of Abu Huraira
D) Hadith of Aisha

Key Takeaway
The sunnah manner of prostration includes placing knees, then hands, then face, and the hadith of Kulayb provides the primary evidence for the sequence.

Rules of Sajdah: Sequence and Posture
Describes the sunnah actions and wajib aspects of prostration in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the wajib and sunnah elements of sajdah, identify the correct sequence of limbs, and cite the Hanafi proof from the hadith of Kulaib.

Core Idea
Standing upright before sajdah is wajib; the sequence and postures are sunnah—all based on authentic hadith.

Prostration (sajdah) is a pillar of prayer, but its precise manner involves both obligatory and recommended actions. In this passage, the commentator expands on the concise text of Al-Wiqayah to clarify that standing upright before sajdah is a wajib (its omission requires sajda sahw) and that the sequence of placing knees before hands is a sunnah proven by the hadith of Kulaib. Additional postures like joining fingers and exposing forearms are also sunnah, each with their own hadith evidence. Understanding these distinctions helps you perform sajdah with full awareness of the legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wajib element
Locate the statement in the commentary that indicates omission requires sajda sahw—that is the standing upright (mustawiyan). This establishes it as wajib.
The commentary says: 'فلو تركه وجب سجود السهو' (if he leaves it, sajda sahw becomes obligatory).
2
Understand the sequence of prostration
The matn states: place knees, then hands, then face. The hadith of Kulaib confirms knees before hands. Compare with the opposing view that places hands first.
The hadith is narrated with a strong chain; Hanafis rely on it as evidence for the sequence.
3
Recognise the sunnah postures during sajdah
The second part of the passage lists sunnah actions: joining fingers, exposing forearms, keeping belly away from thighs, directing toes to qibla, and saying tasbih three times. Each has its own hadith evidence from the commentary.
These are sunnah muakkadah; omission does not require compensation.
4
Note the flexibility in hand position
The commentary mentions that hands can be opposite the ears (narrated by Wail) or opposite the shoulders (narrated by Abu Humayd). The commentator says 'الأمر فيه واسع' (the matter is broad), meaning both are permissible.
This illustrates that variations in sunnah actions are acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقوم مستوياً. ثُمَّ يُكَبِّرُ ويسجد، فيضع ركبتيه أولاً ، ثُمَّ يديه ، ثُمّ وجَهَهُ بين كفيه، ويديه حذاء أذنيه
[1]قوله : ويقوم مستوياً ؛ أي بحيث يستقر كل عضو مكانه ، فلو تركه وجب سجود السهو كذا في «القنية».
[٢] قوله : ويسجد ؛ لم يقل : ساجداً ؛ ليفيد مقارنة التكبير مع السجود، وتنبيهاً على أن ابتداءً التكبير عند ابتداء الانخفاض، وانتهاءه عند وضع جبهته للسجود، صرح به في «المحيط».
[٣] قوله : فيضع ؛ تفسير للسجود ، وبيان لكيفيته الثابتة بحديث كليب له ، قال : «رأیت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا سجد يضع ركبتيه قبل يديه، وإذا نهض رفع يديه قبل ركبتيه» (١) ، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة وأحمد والدارمي وغيرهم بسند قوي.
[٤]قوله : بين كفّيه ؛ كذا روى من فعل الرسول [؟] أخرجه الطحاوي
[5] قوله : حذاء أذنيه ؛ بكسر الحاء ؛ أي مقابل أذنيه، كذا ثبت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برواية وائل (۳) ، أخرجه مسلم والنسائي والطحاوي، وثبت عنها أيضاً برواية أبي حميد الساعدي عند أبي داود والترمذي وضع اليدين حذو المنكبين، والأمر فيه واسع.
ضاماً أصابعه، مبدياً ضبعيه ، مُجافياً بطنَهُ عن فخذيه، موجهاً أصابع رجليه نحو القبلة، ويسبح فيه ثلاثاً.
[١]قوله : ضاماً أصابعه ؛ أي بين اليدين؛ لحديث وائل : «كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا سجد ضم أصابعه»، أخرجه ابن حبان في صحيحه»، والحكمة فيه أنّ الرحمة تنزل حينئذ، فبالضم ينال أكثر.
[٢] قوله : مبدياً ضبعيه ؛ الضبع: بالفتح وسكون الباء : العضد، وقيل: وسطه، وقيل : باطنه، وإبداؤهما تفريجهما، وعدم ضمهما مع الجنبين. كذا في «المغرب»(۲) ، وذلك لحديث: «كان رسول الله الله إذا صلّى فرّج بين يديه حتى يبدو بياض إبطيه»(۳)، أخرجه الشيحان، وعند أبي داودَ: (كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا سجد جافي عضديه عن جنبيه)
[٣] قوله : مجافياً ؛ أي مباعداً بطنه عن كلّ واحدٍ من فخذيه، هو الثابت عن رسول الله عند مسلم والحاكم وأبي يعلى.
[٤] قوله : موجهاً ؛ من التوجيه ؛ أي جاعلاً رؤوس أصابع رجليه إلى القبلة.
And he stands upright. Then he says the takbir and prostrates, and places his knees first, then his hands, then his face between his hands, and his hands opposite his ears. [1] His saying: 'and he stands upright' means such that every limb settles in its place. If he leaves it, sajda sahw becomes obligatory. (Kaza fi al-Qunyah) [2] His saying: 'and prostrates' — he did not say 'prostrating' to indicate the simultaneity of the takbir with the prostration, and as a hint that the beginning of the takbir is at the start of lowering, and its end at placing his forehead for prostration. (Explicit in al-Muhit) [3] His saying: 'places' is an explanation of the prostration and a statement of its manner established by the hadith of Kulaib from him, who said: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, when he prostrated, he placed his knees before his hands, and when he rose, he raised his hands before his knees.' Narrated by the authors of the four Sunan, Ahmad, al-Darimi, and others with a strong chain. [4] His saying: 'between his hands' — thus narrated from the act of the Messenger [unclear] narrated by al-Tahawi. [5] His saying: 'opposite his ears' — with kasra on the ha, meaning opposite his ears. Thus established from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, by the narration of Wail, narrated by Muslim, al-Nasa'i, and al-Tahawi, and also established from him by the narration of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi in Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi placing the hands opposite the shoulders, and the matter is broad. (He joins his fingers together, exposes his forearms, keeps his belly away from his thighs, directs the tips of his toes toward the qibla, and says tasbih three times.) [1] His saying: 'joining his fingers together' means between the hands, based on the hadith of Wail: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, when he prostrated, would join his fingers together.' Narrated by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih. The wisdom is that mercy descends then, and by joining, one receives more. [2] His saying: 'exposing his forearms' — al-dhiba' (with fatha on the da and sukun on the ba) means the upper arm, or its middle, or its inner side. Exposing them means spreading them apart and not bringing them close to the sides. (Kaza fi al-Maghrib) This is based on the hadith: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, when he prayed, would spread his arms so that the whiteness of his armpits became visible.' Narrated by the two Shaykhs. In Abu Dawud: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, when he prostrated, would keep his upper arms away from his sides.' [3] His saying: 'keeping away' means keeping his belly away from each of his thighs. This is established from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, in Muslim, al-Hakim, and Abu Ya'la. [4] His saying: 'directing' — from tawjeeh, meaning making the tips of his toes face the qibla.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah. It details the sunnah actions of prostration (sajdah) in prayer. The base matn gives a concise description: standing upright, then takbir, then placing knees, hands, and face between hands with hands by ears. The commentary first clarifies that 'standing upright' (istawa') is a wajib act—if omitted, one must perform sajda sahw (prostration of forgetfulness). The commentary then explains the wording 'and prostrates' to show that the takbir accompanies the lowering movement, starting as one descends and ending when the forehead touches the ground. The manner of placing the knees before hands is proven by a strong hadith from Kulaib, who saw the Prophet do so. This is the Hanafi position (opposing some other views that hands go first). The hand position is clarified: they can be opposite the ears or shoulders, with flexibility. The second part of the matn describes additional sunnah postures: joining the fingers (to receive more mercy), exposing the forearms (spreading arms so armpits show), keeping the belly away from thighs, and pointing the toes toward the qibla. These are all based on authentic hadith, and the commentary provides the sources. The student should note that while most of these are sunnah (rewardable but not obligatory), the initial standing upright is wajib. The overall description reflects the Hanafi emphasis on following the Prophet's exact practice.

Key Terms
مستوياً (mustawiyan): Standing upright with limbs settled in place; wajib in prayer.
ضَامّاً أَصَابِعَهُ (dāmman aṣābiʿahu): Joining the fingers of both hands together when placing them on the ground in prostration.
مُبْدِياً ضَبْعَيْهِ (mubdiyan ḍabʿayhi): Exposing the forearms/armpits by keeping the arms away from the sides.
مُجَافِياً (mujāfiyan): Keeping the belly away from the thighs during prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on standing upright (istawa') during prayer?
A) It is sunnah; omitting it is permissible without any compensation.
B) It is wajib; omitting it unnecessarily requires sajda sahw.
C) It is fard; omitting it invalidates the prayer.
D) It is mustahab; omitting it is disliked but no compensation needed.

Key Takeaway
Standing upright before sajdah is wajib; the sequence and postures are sunnah—all based on authentic hadith.

Posture in Prostration: Sunnah of Fingers, Arms, Belly, and Feet
Explaining recommended posture during prostration with hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four sunnan postures during prostration and identify the hadith evidence for each.

Core Idea
Prostration posture follows confirmed sunnah — joining fingers, exposing arms, distancing belly, and directing toes to the qiblah.

Prostration (sajdah) is a pillar of prayer, but beyond its obligatory elements, the Prophet ﷺ taught specific postures that perfect the act. This lesson examines four such postures described in the matn and their proofs. Understanding these as sunnah muakkadah helps you perform prayer with greater closeness to the prophetic model without confusing recommendation with obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify each recommended posture
From the matn, extract the four actions: joining fingers, exposing arms, distancing belly from thighs, directing toes to qiblah.
2
Locate the hadith proof for each
For each posture, find the specific hadith cited by the commentator. Note the source (e.g., Ibn Hibban, the Two Shaykhs).
The commentator often uses phrases like 'due to the hadith' or 'this is established from'.
3
Understand the wisdom (hikmah)
The commentator mentions that joining fingers gathers more mercy. Consider how each posture reflects humility and attentiveness in prayer.
Wisdom is supplementary; the ruling is based on action, not necessarily reason.
4
Determine the hukm level
Based on the evidence and Hanafi usul, classify each posture. They are sunnah muakkadah – omission is not sinful but reduces perfection.
Compare with other schools that may consider some as wajib. Hanafis rely on the general command to pray as the Prophet prayed, but classify these as sunnah due to the nature of the evidence.

[1] His statement: 'joining his fingers' means between the hands; due to the hadith of Wa'il: 'The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, when he prostrated, joined his fingers.' Recorded by Ibn Hibban in his Sahih. And the wisdom in it is that mercy descends at that moment, so by joining, one attains more. [2] His statement: 'exposing his upper arms' - ad-dhab' (upper arm) with fathah and sukun of the ba: the upper arm, and it is said: its middle, and it is said: its inner side. This passage is an expansion of the matn describing the recommended posture (sunnah) during prostration. The commentator explains each element with hadith proofs. These are all sunan (recommended), not fard or wajib.

Key Takeaway

Prostration on a Barrier: The Condition of Istiqrār
Ruling on validity of prostration with a barrier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid prostration on a non-earth surface and explain the proof from hadith and Salaf practice.

Core Idea
Prostration is valid on any surface if the forehead settles, regardless of material.

Can you prostrate on your sleeve if the floor is too hot? What about a thick prayer mat? The Hanafi school prioritizes the settling of the forehead over the type of surface. This lesson examines the ruling and its proof from the Prophet ﷺ and the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its key condition
The passage states that prostration on a turban fold, excess garment, or any raised object is valid if the forehead settles (yastaqirru). If it does not settle, it is invalid. This istiqrār is the central condition.
Settling means the forehead rests firmly; it must not sink into the surface (e.g., unpacked snow) or hover (e.g., prostrating in the air).
2
Locate the proof (dalīl) cited
The author cites a hadith about seven bones prostrating (including hands and feet) and specific narrations of the Prophet ﷺ prostrating on his turban and Companions doing so. Also the hadith of Anas about spreading a garment in extreme heat.
These are aḥādīth with weak chains, but the author argues they are strengthened by the widespread practice of the Salaf.
3
Analyze the strength of the proof
The chains are weak (ḍaʿīf), but the author notes that the practice of the Salaf (ʿamal al-salaf) compensates, making the ruling reliable in the Hanafi school. The principle of necessity also supports the dispensation.
This illustrates the Hanafi method of giving weight to ʿamal even when riwāyah is weak.
4
Note the intra-madhhab and cross-madhhab differences
Within the Hanafi school, the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on this ruling. Other schools, especially the Shafiʿi, require the forehead to be directly on earth (turbah) or prohibit any barrier.
The practical difference: Hanafis allow barriers; Shafiʿis do not. The thamarah is the validity of praying on carpet, clothing, etc.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjīḥ
The muftā bihī position is that prostration on any surface is valid if the forehead settles. The proof, though weak individually, is collectively supported by practice and need. The condition of istiqrār ensures the essence of sujūd is preserved.
The ruling is from the ẓāhir al-riwāyah and is relied upon in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

، وكذا أصابع يديه كما في «النقاية» (۱) ؛ لحديث: «إذا سجـد الـعـبـد سـجـد مـعـه سبعة آراب»(۲)، كما مرّ ذكره، وذكر منها : اليدين والرجلين»، فأثبت لها صفة السجود، وهو لا يكون إلا باستقبال القبلة.

[1] قوله : علی گور عمامته ; العمامة بالكسر : ما يلف على الرأس، بالفارسية : دستار، وكورها بالفتح دورها ، يعني : بيج دستار وجواز ذلك لما روي «أنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سجد على كور عمامته (۳) ، أخرجه أبو نعيم في «الحلية»، والطبراني وابن عدي وابن أبي حاتم ، وأسانيده ضعيفة، كما بسطناها في «السعاية». إلا أنه يتقوى بعمل السلف، فقد أخرج البيهقي عن الحسن : «كان أصحاب رسول الله يسجدون وأيديهم في ثيابهم، ويسجد الرجل منهم على كور عمامته»(4).

[٢]قوله : أو فاضل ثوبه ; أي ما فضل من ثوبه الملبوس، كالكم والذيل، فقد عن أنس : كنا نصلي مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في شدة الحر، فإذا لم يستطع أحدنا أن يمكن وجهه من الأرض من شدة الحر بسط ثوبه وسجد عليه).

[٣]قوله : يجد حجمه ; أي رفعته ونتوءه، فلو صلّى على الثلج إن لبده يجوز، الأئمة الستة. روی وإن لم يلبده وكان بحال يغيب فيه وجهه لا يجوز، كالسجدة في الهواء.
If he prostrates on the round of his turban, or on the excess of his garment, or on something whose thickness he feels, and his forehead settles, it is valid; if it does not settle, it is not valid. Likewise his fingers of the hands as in al-Nuqayah; due to the hadith: 'When the servant prostrates, seven bones prostrate with him,' as mentioned before, and among them are the hands and feet, so the description of prostration is established for them, and it (prostration) is only with facing the qiblah. [1] His statement: 'on the round of his turban' - al-‘imamah (turban) is what is wound on the head, in Persian: dastār, and its round (kawr) with fathah is its circle, meaning: the fold of the turban. Permissibility due to what is reported: 'The Prophet prostrated on the round of his turban,' recorded by Abu Nu‘aym in al-Hilyah, al-Tabarani, Ibn ‘Adi, and Ibn Abi Hatim, and its chains are weak, as we detailed in al-Si‘ayah. However, it is strengthened by the practice of the Salaf, for al-Bayhaqi recorded from al-Hasan: 'The Companions of the Messenger of Allah used to prostrate while their hands were in their garments, and a man among them would prostrate on the round of his turban.' [2] His statement: 'or on the excess of his garment' i.e., what is extra from his worn garment, like the sleeve and the tail. For it is reported from Anas: 'We used to pray with the Messenger of Allah in severe heat, and when one of us could not place his face on the ground due to the heat, he would spread his garment and prostrate on it.' [3] His statement: 'he feels its thickness' i.e., its elevation and protrusion. So if he prays on snow, if it is packed, it is valid; if it is not packed and is such that his face sinks into it, it is not valid, like prostrating in the air.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn concerning the validity of prostration when the forehead is on something other than bare earth. The ruling: it is valid (jā'iz) if the forehead settles (yastaqirru) on the surface, even if that surface is the turban, an excess of clothing, or any object with height. The condition is that the forehead must feel the solidity and rest firmly; if it does not settle (e.g., sinking into unpacked snow or prostrating mid-air), the prostration is invalid. The proof includes the Prophet's action and the practice of the Companions. Though the hadith of prostrating on the turban is weak, it is strengthened by the widespread practice of the Salaf. The commentary also reminds that the hands and feet must face the qiblah during sajdah, as per the hadith of the seven bones.

Key Terms
كور عمامة (kawr al-‘imāmah): The round or fold of the turban; the part wound around the head.
فاضل ثوبه (fāḍil thawbihi): The excess part of his garment (e.g., sleeve, tail) which is not the part he is wearing.
يستقر (yastaqirru): Settles; the forehead must rest firmly on the surface, not sink or hover.

Prostration on Another's Back During Crowded Prayer
Ruling on sujood on the back during crowding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sujood on the back of another person during crowding and identify the condition that must be met.

Core Idea
In crowding, prostrate on the back of one praying the same prayer – necessity permits the unusual.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding prostration during congregational prayer when crowding prevents normal sujood. The text clarifies that it is permissible to prostrate on the back of a fellow worshipper, but only if that person is performing the same prayer. The ruling is grounded in the practice of the Companions and provides practical relief in crowded mosque conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the act is (prostration on the back of another) and what hukm level applies (mubah – permissible).
The ruling is conditional: it only applies during crowding.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the statement of Umar (رضي الله عنه) and the narration from Ibn Umar (رضي الله عنهما).
Both are ahad hadith, so the dalil is zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? Umar explicitly permits prostration on the back; Ibn Umar shows the Prophet’s practice.
The permission is explicit in the words 'فليسجد على ظهر أخيه'.
4
Note the condition
Identify the restriction: the person on whose back one prostrates must be performing the same prayer.
The text says 'لا مَن لا يصليها' – not on one who does not pray the same prayer.
5
Evaluate practical implications
Consider how this ruling is applied in crowded mosques today and what the consequences are if the condition is violated.
If violated, the prostration may not count, potentially invalidating the rakah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لو سجد للزّحام على ظَهْرِ مَن يصلّي صلاته، لا مَن لا يصلِّيها. (وكذا لو سجدَ للزِّحام على ظَهْرِ مَن يصلي صلاته، لا مَن لا يصليها) : أي لا على ظهرِ مَن لا يصلي صلاته، وهو إما أن لا يصلّي أصلاً، أو يصلي ولكن لا يصلي صلاته. قوله : وكذا ؛ أي يجوز السجود على ظهر المصلي ؛ لقول عمر رضي الله عنه: «إذا اشتد الزحام فليسجد على ظهر أخيه» ، أخرجه البيهقي، وروي عن ابن عمر : «قرأ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم النجم فسجد فيها فأطال السجود فكثر الناس، فصلى بعضهم على ظهر» ، كذا قال ابن حَجَر له في «تلخيص الحبير»
Similarly, if he prostrates due to crowding on the back of one who prays his prayer, not one who does not pray it. (Similarly, if he prostrates due to crowding on the back of one who prays his prayer, not one who does not pray it): i.e., not on the back of one who does not pray his prayer, and that is either one who does not pray at all, or one who prays but not his prayer. His saying: 'Similarly' means: It is permissible to prostrate on the back of a praying person due to the saying of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'When crowding becomes severe, let him prostrate on the back of his brother' – narrated by al-Bayhaqi. And it is narrated from Ibn Umar: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) recited Surah An-Najm and prostrated in it and prolonged the prostration, so many people came and some of them prayed on [his] back' – thus said Ibn Hajar in his 'Talkhis al-Habir'.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning prostration on the back of another person during congregational prayer when there is crowding. The base text (matn) states that it is permissible to prostrate on the back of someone who is performing the same prayer (i.e., following the same imam or parallel prayer), but not on the back of someone who is not praying the same prayer, either because they are not in prayer at all or are performing a different prayer. The commentary clarifies this distinction and provides evidence: the statement of Umar ibn al-Khattab (رضي الله عنه) allowing prostration on the back of a brother, and a narration from Ibn Umar (رضي الله عنهما) reporting that during the Prophet’s (ﷺ) long prostration while reciting Surah An-Najm, people prayed on the Prophet’s back. This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh and is based on the principle of necessity during crowding.

Key Terms
الزحام (al-ziham): Crowding of worshippers in congregational prayer that prevents performing sujood normally.
السجود على ظهر المصلي (al-sujud 'ala zahr al-musalli): Prostration on the back of a person who is also praying the same prayer, valid during crowding.
صلاة (salat): Prayer; in context, refers to a specific prayer that is the same as the one being performed by the one prostrating.

Key Takeaway

Women's Posture in Sujood (Sunnah of Lowering and Compressing)
Ruling on women lowering and sticking belly to thighs in prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the recommended posture for a woman in sujood according to the Hanafi school and cite the prophetic evidence for it.

Core Idea
A woman in sujood lowers herself and sticks her belly to her thighs for maximum modesty — distinct from a man's posture.

This lesson addresses the specific posture a woman should adopt during prostration (sujood). While men are instructed to keep their limbs raised and separated, women are to compress themselves, sticking their belly to their thighs. This directive is rooted in prophetic guidance emphasizing concealment and modesty. We will examine the textual evidence and understand the reasoning behind this sunnah practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the woman's posture in sujood: she should lower herself and stick her belly to her thighs. The ruling is sunnah (recommended), not fard or wajib.
Ruling level from Hanafi position box: sunnah.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
Two hadith are cited: a mursal narration from Abu Dawud and a marfu' narration from al-Bayhaqi. Both instruct women to press their flesh to the ground and describe the most concealing posture.
Hadith texts are provided in the source.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the evidence proves the ruling)
The instruction 'press some of the flesh to the ground' directly implies compressing limbs. The description 'stick her belly to her thighs' specifies the method. The expression 'most concealing' indicates the reasoning: modesty (satr).
Wajh al-dalalah: direct command and explicit description.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i, Maliki) may not require the same degree of compression, considering the general sujood posture sufficient. The Hanafi view is based on these specific ahadith.
Cross-madhhab status: mukhtalaf fih.
5
Evaluate the authority (thubut and dalalah)
Both hadith are ahad (zanni al-thubut) and their meaning is clear (zanni al-dalalah). However, they are accepted by Hanafi scholars as sufficient basis for a sunnah ruling.
Authority weight: zahir riwayah; recommended practice.

And the woman lowers herself and sticks her belly to her thighs. His saying: 'The woman lowers herself' meaning she lowers down and does not raise her limbs, and 'sticks' meaning she attaches her belly to her thighs. The basis for this is what Abu Dawud narrated in his Mursal: 'The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) passed by two women praying and said: "When you prostrate, press some of the flesh to the ground."' Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a woman's posture during prostration. Unlike men who keep their limbs raised and separated, a woman is to keep her limbs together, lowering her body and sticking her belly to her thighs. This is based on prophetic instructions that ensure maximum concealment (satr). The hadith from Abu Dawud (mursal) instructs women to press their flesh to the ground, and al-Bayhaqi's marfu' narration explicitly says sticking the belly to the thighs is most concealing. The commentator mentions he has elaborated on other gender-differentiated rulings in his work 'al-Si'ayah.'

Key Terms
تنخفض (tanakhfadu): The woman lowers herself without raising her limbs, opposite to a man's posture.
تلزق (talzuqu): She sticks her belly to her thighs, achieving maximum concealment.
