Standing from Sujood: Hanafi Ruling and the Jalsat al-Istirahah Dispute
Ruling on standing without support and the Shafi'i disagreement on sitting briefly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on standing from sujood without hand support, explain the Shafi'i position on jalsat al-istirahah, and evaluate the strength of each dalil.

Core Idea
No sitting pause (jalsat al-istirahah) after sujood; standing directly without hand support is the Hanafi practice.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi ruling on how to rise from prostration to the next rak'ah. The school instructs standing upright without using the hands for support and without any brief sitting. The commentator then introduces a disagreement: Imam al-Shafi'i recommends a short sitting pause (jalsat al-istirahah). Let us walk through the evidence and reasoning that underpin this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Hanafi Ruling
Identify the two commands in the matn: stand upright (mustawiyan) and without support (bila i'timad) and without sitting (la qu'ud). Understand that 'bila i'timad' means no hand support.
The ruling applies when moving from the second prostration to standing for the next rak'ah.
2
Analyze the Karahah Degree
Note that using hand support is makruh tanzihi (non-prohibitive dislike). The text states it is disliked but not sinful.
Karahah tanzihiyyah is a lighter level; the act is valid but leaving it is better.
3
Identify the Opposing School's Position
State the Shafi'i view: they recommend sitting briefly (jalsat al-istirahah) and using hand support when rising, based on the hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith.
The Shafi'i consider this as a sunnah act.
4
Compare the Dalil Used by Each School
Hanafi dalil: hadith of Abu Hurayrah (Prophet rose on tops of his feet) plus practice of many Companions. Shafi'i dalil: hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith (Prophet sat and supported on hands).
Note the Hanafi argument that the slight weakness in the chain of Abu Hurayrah's hadith is compensated by the practice of major Companions.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: in Hanafi prayer, no sitting pause; in Shafi'i prayer, a short sit is recommended. This affects the sequence of movements in salah.
The disagreement is about a recommended act, not a pillar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقوم مستوياً بلا اعتماد على الأرض، ولا قعود، وفيه خلافُ الشَّافِعِيِّ جلسة الاستراحة ويسمى. قوله: بلا اعتماد؛ أي لا يعتمد بيديه على الأرض عند القيام، كاعتمادِ العاجز الضعيف؛ لأنَّ النبي ﷺ نهى عن ذلك، أخرجه أبو داود، والكراهة هاهنا تنزيهية، فإن اعتمد فلا بأس به، كما في «المحيط». قوله: خلاف الشافعي؛ فإنّه يقول بأفضلية الاعتماد والقعود أخذاً من حديث مالك بن الحويرث أنه قال: «أريكم صلاة رسول الله ﷺ فصلى فإذا رفع رأسه عن السجدة الثانية جلس واعتمد على الأرض»، أخرجه البخاري، وأخرج القعود عنه أصحاب السنن أيضاً. ولنا: ما أخرجه الترمذي عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه: أنّ رسول الله ﷺ كان ينهض في الصلاة على صدور قدميه، وفي سنده ضعف يسير ينجبر بعمل أكابر الصحابة؛ كابن مسعود وابن عمر وابن الزبير وعمرو وعلي وابن عبّاس وأبي سعيد الخدري وغيرهم، فإنهم كانوا لا يجلسون جلسة الاستراحة كما أخرجه ابن أبي شَيْبَة.
And he stands upright, without support on the ground, and without sitting. And there is the dispute of al-Shafi'i regarding the sitting of rest (jalsat al-istirahah). His saying: 'without support' means he does not support himself with his hands on the ground when standing, like the support of the weak and helpless, because the Prophet (ﷺ) forbade that, narrated by Abu Dawud. And the dislike here is tanzihi (recommended dislike); if he supports himself, there is no harm, as in 'al-Muhit'. His saying: 'the dispute of al-Shafi'i' for he says that using support and sitting is superior, taking from the hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith who said: 'I show you the prayer of the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ): he prayed, and when he raised his head from the second prostration, he sat and supported himself on the ground' – narrated by al-Bukhari. And the companions of the Sunan also narrated the sitting. And our proof is what al-Tirmidhi narrated from Abu Hurayrah (رضي الله عنه) that the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) used to rise in prayer on the tops of his feet, and in its chain there is slight weakness that is compensated by the practice of the great Companions: Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Umar, Ibn al-Zubayr, Amr, Ali, Ibn Abbas, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and others, for they did not sit for the jalsat al-istirahah, as narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage addresses the manner of standing up from prostration. The Hanafi ruling is to stand upright without using the hands for support and without sitting briefly (jalsat al-istirahah). The prohibition against using hands is based on a hadith in Abu Dawud, but the dislike is tanzihi – meaning it is not sinful to do so, but it is better to avoid. The commentator then presents the Shafi'i view, which recommends using hand support and sitting briefly before standing, based on the hadith of Malik ibn al-Huwayrith. The Hanafi response is that the proof for not sitting is from the hadith of Abu Hurayrah that the Prophet used to rise on the tops of his feet, and that even though its chain is slightly weak, it is strengthened by the consistent practice of many major Companions who did not perform the jalsat al-istirahah. Thus, the Hanafi school adopts the view of not sitting.

Key Terms
جلسة الاستراحة (jalsat al-istirahah): A brief sitting pause after the second prostration before standing, which the Shafi'i school recommends and Hanafi does not.
كراهة تنزيهية (karahah tanzihiyyah): A degree of dislike that is not prohibitive; doing the action is not sinful but leaving it is better.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is forbidden (haram)
B) It is disliked but not sinful (makruh tanzihi)
C) It is recommended (sunnah)
D) It is obligatory (wajib)

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, standing from sujood should be done without hand support and without the jalsat al-istirahah; using support is permissible but disliked.

The Hanafi Position on Leaning and Jalsat al-Istiraha
Ruling on standing in prayer and the Shafi'i dispute regarding jalsat al-istiraha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on leaning and the resting sitting in prayer, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between the two schools.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school avoids the resting sitting; it is not part of the sunnah of prayer.

Standing in prayer is a pillar, but how one stands can vary. Should you lean on the ground or sit briefly before rising? The Hanafi school says no, while the Shafi'i school says yes. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the practical difference between the two positions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi ruling is that one should stand straight without leaning on the hands or ground, and without performing the brief sitting (jalsat al-istiraha) after the second prostration. Leaning is makruh tanzih (disliked but not prohibited).
The key term is 'i'timad' (leaning for support).
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i considers leaning and the resting sitting as preferable (mustahab), based on the hadith of Malik ibn Huwayrith who described the Prophet doing so.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i hadith is authentic (recorded by Bukhari) and explicitly shows the Prophet sitting and leaning after the second prostration.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Abu Hurayra about rising on the soles, strengthened by the practice of senior companions. The Shafi'i hadith is interpreted as indicating permissibility (jawaz) or for those with an excuse, not as a sunnah.
The Hanafi response uses tafsir of the evidence, not rejection.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Shafi'i may perform the resting sitting as a recommended act; a Hanafi avoids it (makruh tanzih) and may not do it.
Both schools agree the prayer is valid either way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقوم مستوياً بلا اعتماد على الأرض، ولا قعود)، وفيه خلافُ الشَّافِعِيِّ جلسة الاستراحة ويسمى [1] [2] [1]قوله : بلا اعتماد ؛ أي لا يعتمد بيديه على الأرض عند القيام، كاعتمادِ العاجز الضعيف ؛ لأنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى عن ذلك [2] أخرجه أبو داود، والكراهة هاهنا تنزيهية، فإن اعتمد فلا بأس به، كما في «المحيط». [2]قوله : خلاف الشافعي ؛ فإنّه يقول بأفضلية الاعتماد والقعود أخذاً من حديث مالك بن الحويرث أنه قال : «أريكم صلاة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فصلى فإذا رفع رأسه عن السجدة الثانية جلس واعتمد على الأرض» [3] ، أخرجه البخاري، وأخرج القعود عنه أصحاب السنن أيضاً [4] . ولنا : ما أخرجه الترمذي عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه : أنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان ينهض في الصلاة على صدور قدميه [5] ، وفي سنده ضعف يسير ينجبر بعمل أكابر الصحابة ؛ كابن مسعود وابن عمر وابن الزبير وعمرو وعلي وابن عبّاس وأبي سعيد الخدري [6] وغيرهم ، فإنهم كانوا لا يجلسون جلسة الاستراحة كما أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة والبيهقي [1] ، وحديث مالك محمول على بيان الجواز أو حالة العذر ونحو ذلك، كما حققه ابن القيم في «زاد المعاد في هدي خير العباد [2].
And he stands straight without leaning on the ground, and without sitting. In it is the disagreement of al-Shafi'i regarding the resting sitting. [1] His saying: 'without leaning' means he does not support himself with his hands on the ground when standing, like the support of the weak and incapacitated; because the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, forbade that. Abu Dawood recorded it. And the dislikedness here is tanzihi (disliked but not prohibited); if he does lean, there is no harm, as in al-Muhit. [2] His saying: 'disagreement of al-Shafi'i' for he says that leaning and sitting [briefly] are better, taking from the hadith of Malik ibn Huwayrith who said: 'I will show you the prayer of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, so he prayed, and when he raised his head from the second prostration, he sat and leaned on the ground.' Al-Bukhari recorded it, and the compilers of Sunan also recorded the sitting from him. Our proof: what al-Tirmidhi recorded from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah used to rise in prayer on the soles of his feet. Its chain has slight weakness, but it is strengthened by the practice of the senior Companions: Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Umar, Ibn al-Zubayr, Amr, Ali, Ibn Abbas, Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, and others, for they did not practice the resting sitting, as recorded by Ibn Abi Shayba and al-Bayhaqi. The hadith of Malik is interpreted as indicating permissibility or a case of excuse and the like, as verified by Ibn Qayyim in Zad al-Ma'ad.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of standing in prayer. The matn states that the person praying should stand straight, without leaning on the ground with the hands, and without performing the brief sitting known as jalsat al-istiraha. The commentator first explains that leaning is disliked (makruh tanzih), but if one does it, the prayer is still valid. Then he presents the Shafi'i view, which considers leaning and the resting sitting preferable based on the hadith of Malik ibn Huwayrith. The Hanafi response is that the hadith of Abu Hurayra about rising on the soles is stronger, especially since it is supported by the practice of many senior Companions. The Shafi'i hadith is interpreted as indicating only permissibility, not preference, or it applies to an excuse. Thus, the Hanafi position is that the resting sitting is not part of the sunnah; rather, one should stand directly without sitting after the second prostration of the first rak'ah.

Key Terms
جلسة الاستراحة (jalsat al-istiraha): The brief sitting after the second prostration of the first rak'ah before standing for the second rak'ah.
اعتماد (i'timad): Leaning on the hands or ground for support when standing in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is makruh tanzih; the prayer is still valid.
C) It is recommended (mustahab).
D) It is wajib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that one should stand straight without leaning or sitting briefly, and this is the mufta bihi.

Omission of Thana' and Ta'awwudh in the Second Rak'ah
Ruling with proof: omission of thana' and ta'awwudh in second rak'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on thana' and ta'awwudh in the second rak'ah, explain the dalil from the Prophet's practice, and evaluate the cross-madhhab position on ta'awwudh.

Core Idea
Thana' and ta'awwudh are recited only in the first rak'ah; the Prophet began the second rak'ah with recitation without any silence.

This lesson clarifies that the second rak'ah is identical to the first in its sunnah and wajibat except that the opening supplication (thana') and the seeking of refuge (ta'awwudh) are not recited before al-Fatiha. The commentary cites an authentic hadith from Abu Hurayra showing the Prophet began recitation immediately upon standing for the second rak'ah, and discusses the general verse from Surah al-Nahl which might suggest istihbab in every rak'ah, but the transmitted practice overrides it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the precise ruling
The matn states that the second rak'ah is like the first except no thana' and no ta'awwudh. Note what exactly is omitted: the opening supplication 'Subhanaka' and the refuge-seeking phrase.
The phrase 'la thana'' includes both thana' and ta'awwudh.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith from Abu Hurayra (in Sahih Muslim and others) reports that when the Prophet stood for the second rak'ah, he began recitation without any silence. This absence of silence indicates no thana' is recited, as thana' would require a pause.
The commentary also references Zad al-Ma'ad and other works.
3
Understand the reasoning
One opening supplication suffices for the entire prayer because there is no true break between the rak'ahs; the taslim is not yet pronounced. Dhikr between recitations does not count as a separation.
This reasoning is analogous to a single recitation interrupted by dhikr.
4
Examine the verse on ta'awwudh
The general command in al-Nahl 98 ('So when you recite the Quran, seek refuge...') could be read as recommending ta'awwudh before every recitation. However, the established practice of the Prophet (not doing so in later rak'ahs) limits its application.
The Hanafi position follows the actual practice over the general implication.
5
Compare with other schools
The Shafi'i school and others consider the verse general and recommend ta'awwudh in every rak'ah. The Hanafi school's reliance on the hadith of immediate recitation leads to omission.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والركعة الثانية كالأولى لكن لا ثناء، ولا تعوذ [1] قوله : لا ثناء؛ أي لا يقرأ فيها : سبحانك اللهم وبحمدك في الركعة الثانية قبل القراءة، فإنّه مختص بالركعة الأولى؛ للحديث الصحيح الوارد في الصحاح عن أبي هريرة: «إنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان إذا نهض من الركعة الثانية استفتح القراءة، ولم يسكت [3] . وإنما يكفي استفتاح واحد؛ لأنه لم يتخلل بين القراءتين سكوت، بل ذكر، فهي كالقراءة الواحدة إذا تخللها حمد الله أو تسبيح والتهليل أو صلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم . كذا في زاد المعاد في هدي خير العباد». [2]قوله : ولا تعوّذ؛ يؤخذ ذلك من الحديث المذكور آنفاً، وقد اشتهر عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه كان يتعوذ قبل القراءة في الركعة الأولى [5] فيما أخرجه أحمد وأصحاب السننِ الأربعة والحاكم وابن حبّان وغيرهم، ولم ينقل تعوّذه في باقي الركعات، وعموم قوله : (فَإِذَا قَرَأْتَ الْقُرْآنَ فَاسْتَعِذْ بِاللهِ مِنَ الشَّيْطَانِ الرَّجِيمِ ) (النحل : 98) ، يقتضي استحبابه في كلّ ركعة. كذا في «تلخيص الحبير» للحافظ ابن حجر.
And the second rak'ah is like the first except that there is no thana' (opening supplication) and no ta'awwudh (seeking refuge). [1] His saying: 'no thana'' means he does not recite in it 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bi hamdika' in the second rak'ah before recitation; for it is specific to the first rak'ah, because of the authentic hadith reported in the Sahih collections from Abu Hurayra: 'When the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, stood up for the second rak'ah, he began recitation and did not remain silent.' And one opening supplication is sufficient because there is no silence between the two recitations, but rather dhikr; it is like a single recitation if praise, glorification, or salutation on the Prophet intervenes. As in Zad al-Ma'ad. [2] His saying: 'and no ta'awwudh' is derived from the hadith mentioned earlier; it is well-known from the Messenger of Allah that he used to seek refuge before recitation in the first rak'ah, as recorded by Ahmad, the four Sunan authors, al-Hakim, Ibn Hibban, and others, and it is not reported that he sought refuge in the remaining rak'ahs. The general meaning of His statement: 'So when you recite the Quran, seek refuge with Allah from the accursed Satan' (al-Nahl: 98) indicates its recommendation (istihbab) in every rak'ah. As in Talkhis al-Habir.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage explains the second rak'ah's recitation rules. The matn says the second rak'ah is like the first except without thana' (the opening supplication 'Subhanaka') and without ta'awwudh (seeking refuge). The commentator clarifies that thana' is only for the first rak'ah, based on the hadith that the Prophet began recitation immediately upon standing for the second rak'ah without silence. This indicates no thana' is required; one opening supplication at the start of the prayer suffices. For ta'awwudh, the established practice is that the Prophet sought refuge only before the first rak'ah. The general verse from Surah al-Nahl implies istihbab (recommendation) in every rak'ah, but since the Prophet did not do so, the Hanafi position is to follow the transmitted practice and omit it in later rak'ahs. The permissibility of reciting it, however, is left open.

Key Terms
ثناء (thana'): The opening supplication 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bi hamdika' recited at the start of the first rak'ah.
تعوذ (ta'awwudh): The phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytan al-rajim' seeking refuge before reciting Quran.
استفتاح (istiftah): The opening supplication in prayer, synonymous with thana' in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the Prophet began the second rak'ah by reciting the Quran without any silence.
C) Because the second rak'ah is shorter.
D) Because thana' is only for the imam.

Key Takeaway
Thana' and ta'awwudh are sunnah only in the first rak'ah; the Prophet's practice of immediate recitation in the second rak'ah is the primary evidence.

Rulings of the Second Rak'ah: Omissions and Sitting
Explanation of rulings specific to the second rak'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three omissions specific to the second rak'ah according to the Hanafi school and identify the evidence for each, and explain the dispute over sitting posture.

Core Idea
The second rak'ah omits thana', ta'awwudh, and raising hands; sitting after it is iftirash, not tawarruk.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the distinctive rulings of the second rak'ah as defined in al-Wiqaya. While the second rak'ah largely mirrors the first, three elements are specifically omitted, and the sitting posture after it follows a different sunnah. The commentator also addresses an intra-Hanafi difference regarding ta'awwudh and a cross-madhhab dispute about the sitting posture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three omissions
List the three elements omitted in the second rak'ah: thana', ta'awwudh, and raising hands. Note that these are omitted by sunnah, not wajib.
The matn explicitly states 'la thana', wa la ta'awwudh, wa la raf' yadayhi'.
2
Analyze the evidence for omitting thana' and ta'awwudh
Examine the hadith of Abu Hurayra (Muslim) showing the Prophet began recitation directly after rising, without pausing. For ta'awwudh, note the absence of reports of him seeking refuge in later rak'ahs, but the general verse (16:98) suggests otherwise.
The commentator cites Talkhis al-Habir for the verse argument.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference on ta'awwudh
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad recommend ta'awwudh in every rak'ah because recitation occurs anew. Understand why the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows the matn and omits it after the first.
This shows usul principle: general verse vs. specific practice.
4
Understand the sitting posture after the second rak'ah
Recognize that the matn says 'iftarasha' (iftirash). The evidence comes from hadiths of Aisha and Ibn Umar describing the Prophet sitting on his left foot with the right erect.
The commentator mentions a treatise by Qasim ibn Qutlubugha collecting these reports.
5
Explore the cross-madhhab dispute on sitting
Shafi'i and others rely on the detailed hadith of Abu Humayd specifying iftirash in the first sitting and tawarruk in the final. Understand the usul principle: al-mufassal qadin 'ala al-mujmal. The commentator calls this 'the most just view' but Hanafi fatwa remains with iftirash in all sittings.
This is the main analytical point: general vs. specific evidence.

كتاب الصلاة صفة الصلاة والركعة الثانية كالأولى لكن لا ثناء، ولا تعوذ، ولا رفع يديه فيها، وإذا أتمها افترش رجله اليُسرى، وجلس عليها ناصباً يُمناه. [شرح: قوله : لا ثناء؛ أي لا يقرأ فيها: سبحانك اللهم وبحمدك في الركعة الثانية قبل القراءة، فإنّه مختص بالركعة الأولى؛ للحديث الصحيح الوارد في الصحاح عن أبي هريرة: «إنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان إذا نهض من الركعة الثانية استفتح القراءة، ولم يسكت». وإنما يكفي استفتاح واحد؛ لأنه لم يتخلل بين القراءتين سكوت، بل ذكر، فهي كالقراءة الواحدة إذا تخللها حمد الله أو تسبيح والتهليل أو صلاة على النبي. كذا في زاد المعاد. قوله : ولا تعوّذ؛ يؤخذ ذلك من الحديث المذكور آنفاً، وقد اشتهر عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه كان يتعوذ قبل القراءة في الركعة الأولى فيما أخرجه أحمد وأصحاب السننِ الأربعة والحاكم وابن حبّان وغيرهم، ولم ينقل تعوّذه في باقي الركعات، وعموم قوله: (فَإِذَا قَرَأْتَ الْقُرْآنَ فَاسْتَعِذْ بِاللهِ مِنَ الشَّيْطَانِ الرَّجِيمِ) يقتضي استحبابه في كلّ ركعة. كذا في «تلخيص الحبير». وفي «حَلْبة المجلّي» لابن أمير حاج: «ينبغي على قول أبي يوسف ومحمد أن يتعوذ في الثانية أيضاً، فإنّه إنما شرع للقراءة، والقراءة تتجدد في كل ركعة». قوله : ولا رفع يديه؛ وذلك لأنّ رفع اليدين في الأولى كان للاستفتاح، ولا استفتاح في غيرها، فلا ترفع الأيدي فيهما. قوله : افترش رجله؛ بكسر الراء: أي جعله مفروشاً وموضوعاً على الأرض وجلس عليها؛ لحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يفرش رجله اليسرى وينصب اليمنى، أخرجه مسلم، وعند النسائي عن ابن عمر الله: «من سنة الصلاة أن تنصب القدم اليمنى، واستقباله بأصابعه إلى القبلة، والجلوس على اليسرى». وفي الباب أخبار وآثار أخر أيضاً بسطها قاسم بن قُطلُوبُغا في رسالته: «الأسوس في كيفية الجلوس»، وبإطلاقها أخذ أصحابنا، فجعلوا هذه الكيفية سنة في جميع جلسات الصلوات. وقد ورد أحاديث مفصلة مصرحة بأنّ جلوسه على طريق الافتراش كان في القعدة الأولى وغيرها، وفي الأخيرة كان على سبيل التورك، أخرجه البخاري وأصحاب السنن وغيرهم، وبه أخذ الشافعي وغيره، بناءً على أن المفصل قاض على المجمل، وهو القول الأعدل. وأما استنان التورّك في الأولى أيضاً كما حكي عن مالك له، فليس له أصل يعتد به.
Book of Prayer, Description of Prayer: The second rak'ah is like the first, but there is no thana', no ta'awwudh, and no raising of the hands in it. And when he completes it, he spreads his left foot and sits on it, erecting his right foot. [Commentary: His statement: 'no thana'' means he does not recite 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bihamdika' in the second rak'ah before recitation, for it is specific to the first rak'ah due to the authentic hadith in the Sahih works from Abu Hurayra: 'The Prophet, when he rose from the second rak'ah, began recitation and did not pause.' One opening supplication suffices because there is no silence between the two recitations but rather remembrance, so it is like a single recitation when interrupted by praising Allah or tasbih or tahlil or sending blessings on the Prophet. As in Zad al-Ma'ad. His statement: 'no ta'awwudh'; this is taken from the aforementioned hadith. It is well-known that the Messenger of Allah used to seek refuge before recitation in the first rak'ah, as recorded by Ahmad, the four Sunan compilers, al-Hakim, Ibn Hibban and others, and he is not reported to have sought refuge in the remaining rak'ahs. The general verse 'When you recite the Qur'an, seek refuge with Allah from the accursed Satan' implies its desirability in every rak'ah. As in Talkhis al-Habir. In Halbat al-Mujalli by Ibn Amir Hajj: 'It is appropriate, according to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, to seek refuge in the second as well, for it is prescribed for recitation, and recitation occurs anew in every rak'ah.' His statement: 'no raising of the hands'; because raising the hands in the first was for the opening supplication, and there is no opening supplication in other rak'ahs, so hands are not raised in them. His statement: 'he spreads his foot'; meaning he makes it spread out and placed on the ground and sits on it; based on the hadith of Aisha: 'The Messenger of Allah used to spread his left foot and erect his right foot.' Reported by Muslim. And according to al-Nasa'i from Ibn Umar: 'It is from the sunnah of prayer to erect the right foot, face its toes towards the qibla, and sit on the left foot.' There are other reports and traditions on this topic which Qasim ibn Qutlubugha expanded in his treatise 'al-Asus fi Kayfiyyat al-Julus'. Our Companions took these reports generally and made this manner sunnah in all sittings of prayers. Detailed hadiths have been reported specifying that his sitting in the manner of iftirash was in the first sitting and others, while in the last sitting it was in the manner of tawarruk. Reported by al-Bukhari and the Sunan compilers and others. Al-Shafi'i and others adopted this, based on the principle that the detailed overrides the general, and this is the most just view. As for regarding tawarruk as sunnah in the first sitting as well, as related from Malik, it has no reliable basis.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya, explaining the rulings specific to the second rak'ah of prayer. The author first establishes that the second rak'ah mirrors the first except for three omissions: thana' (the opening supplication 'Subhanaka'), ta'awwudh (seeking refuge), and raising the hands. The proof for omitting thana' is the hadith that the Prophet (ﷺ) began recitation directly upon rising from the second rak'ah without a pause, indicating that reopening supplication is not required. The author analogizes this to a single recitation interrupted by dhikr. For ta'awwudh, the hadith evidence that the Prophet (ﷺ) sought refuge only before the first rak'ah is cited, though the general verse (16:98) suggests it is recommended in every rak'ah. The commentator notes an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that ta'awwudh is recommended in every rak'ah because it is tied to recitation. However, the relied-upon Hanafi position (as per the matn) is that it is omitted in the second and subsequent rak'ahs. Raising hands is omitted because it is only for the opening takbir and thana', which do not recur. The second part discusses the sitting posture after the second rak'ah: iftirash (sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect). This is established by multiple hadiths, including those of Aisha and Ibn Umar. The Hanafi school applies this to all sittings (first tashahhud and final tashahhud). However, the commentator notes a detailed hadith specifying that the Prophet (ﷺ) sat in iftirash in the first sitting and tawarruk (sitting on the left hip) in the final sitting. Imam al-Shafi'i and others adopted this distinction, arguing that the detailed report overrides the general. The commentator considers this the more just view ('aqwal ad'fal), but the Hanafi school generally follows the general reports, making iftirash sunnah in all sittings. The view attributed to Malik that tawarruk is sunnah in the first sitting is dismissed as baseless.

Key Terms
ثناء (thanā'): The opening supplication 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bihamdika' recited at the beginning of the first rak'ah.
تعوذ (taʿawwudh): The phrase 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytan al-rajim' seeking refuge from Satan before recitation.
افتراش (iftirāsh): Sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect and toes facing qibla.
تورك (tawarruk): Sitting on the left hip with both feet protruding to the right, typically in the final tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Saying 'Subhanaka Allahumma wa bihamdika' (thana')
C) Saying 'Allahu Akbar' for ruku
D) Sitting for tashahhud

Key Takeaway
The second rak'ah omits the opening supplication, seeking refuge, and raising hands; the sunnah sitting is iftirash, though a strong alternate view exists for tawarruk in the final sitting.

Raising Hands and Reciting Ta'awwudh in Prayer
Explains rulings on hand-raising and ta'awwudh with reasoning and intra-madhhab difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raising hands in prayer and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding ta'awwudh recitation.

Core Idea
In Hanafi, raising hands is only at the opening takbir; ta'awwudh is recommended in every rak'ah by two imams but the relied view limits it to the first.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains two key salah rulings: first, that hands are raised only at the opening takbir, and second, that there is an internal disagreement among Hanafi imams on reciting ta'awwudh in every rak'ah. Understanding these rulings clarifies common misconceptions and the school's reliance on transmitted practice and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts and their rulings
First, raise hands only at takbirat al-ihram (sunnah). Second, recite ta'awwudh (seeking refuge) — the text mentions a difference: Abu Hanifa limits it to the first rak'ah, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad recommend it in every rak'ah.
Both rulings are about sunnah acts, not fard or wajib.
2
Locate the reasoning (ta'lil)
For hand-raising: it is specific to the opening supplication (istiftah), which occurs only once. For ta'awwudh: it is prescribed for recitation, which renews in each rak'ah.
The reasoning connects the act to its purpose as transmitted in the sunnah.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on ta'awwudh
Abu Hanifa restricts ta'awwudh to the first rak'ah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it recommended in every rak'ah because recitation occurs in each.
This is an internal disagreement within the school, not between madhhabs.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view for raising hands is only at the opening takbir. For ta'awwudh, the mufta bihi is to recite it only in the first rak'ah, following Abu Hanifa.
The view of the Sahibayn is recorded in the Nawadir, but the Zahir al-Riwayah supports the restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا رفع يديه فيها. قوله : ولا رفع يديه ؛ وذلك لأنّ رفع اليدين في الأولى كان للاستفتاح، ولا استفتاح في غيرها، فلا ترفع الأيدي فيهما. وفي «حَلْبة المجلّي» لابن أمير حاج : «ينبغي على قول أبي يوسف ومحمد الله أن يتعوذ في الثانية أيضاً، فإنّه إنما شرع للقراءة ، والقراءة تتجدد في كل ركعة».
And he does not raise his hands in it. He said: 'And do not raise his hands'; that is because raising the hands in the first (takbir) was for the opening supplication, and there is no opening supplication in other parts, so hands are not raised in them. And in Halbat al-Mujalli by Ibn Amir Haj: 'It is appropriate according to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) that he should seek refuge (ta'awwudh) in the second rak'ah as well, because it was legislated for recitation, and recitation is renewed in every rak'ah.'
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings. First, the matn states that hands are not raised during the prayer except at the beginning. The commentator explains the reasoning: raising hands is specific to the opening takbir (istiftah), and since there is no istiftah elsewhere, hands should not be raised. This is a Hanafi position based on the principle that acts of worship are restricted to transmitted practice. Second, the commentator quotes from Halbat al-Mujalli that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, it is recommended to recite ta'awwudh (seeking refuge) in the second rak'ah as well as the first. Their reasoning is that ta'awwudh is prescribed for recitation, and recitation occurs in every rak'ah. The text does not specify the mufta bihi position, but in the Hanafi school, the relied-upon view is that ta'awwudh is said only in the first rak'ah. This difference is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa (who limits it to the first) and his two disciples.

Key Terms
رفع اليدين (raf' al-yadayn): Raising the hands during prayer; specifically at the opening takbir according to this text.
التعوذ (al-ta'awwudh): Seeking refuge with Allah from Satan before reciting Quran in prayer.
الاستفتاح (al-istiftah): The opening supplication said after the first takbir before Fatiha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Sitting Posture in Prayer: Iftirash vs Tawarruk
Discusses the Hanafi position of iftirash and the Shafi'i view of tawarruk in the final sitting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on sitting posture, explain the Shafi'i objection based on detailed hadith, and evaluate the Hanafi tarjih.

Core Idea
Hanafis hold iftirash in all sittings; Shafi'is differentiate with tawarruk in the final tashahhud.

This lesson examines a classic ikhtilaf over the sunnah posture during the tashahhud. The Hanafi school relies on general hadith to prescribe iftirash in every sitting, while the Shafi'i school follows detailed narrations that specify tawarruk for the final sitting. Understanding how each school handles the tension between general and specific texts is key to appreciating their differing rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that iftirash (sitting on the left foot, erecting the right) is sunnah in both the first and final tashahhud.
This is based on general hadith from Aisha and Ibn Umar, taken as universal.
2
Identify the Hanafi evidence
The general hadith (e.g., Muslim, Nasa'i) describe the Prophet sitting in iftirash without specifying the sitting, so Hanafis apply it to all sittings.
Hanafis give priority to the generality (umum) of the text.
3
State the opposing school and its evidence
Imam al-Shafi'i and others cite detailed hadith (e.g., Abu Humayd in Bukhari) that specify iftirash in the first tashahhud and tawarruk in the final one.
Shafi'is argue that the specific (mufassal) overrides the general (mujmal).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi commentator acknowledges the detailed hadith and even calls the Shafi'i view 'the most balanced', but the Hanafi school retains iftirash in all sittings, treating the general hadith as sufficient and not contradicted.
The Hanafi response does not rebut the detailed hadith but adheres to the general as a valid basis.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, a Hanafi sits in iftirash after every two rak'ahs and at the end; a Shafi'i sits in iftirash after the first two rak'ahs and in tawarruk after the last.
The difference affects all prayers with multiple tashahhuds (Maghrib, Isha, Zuhr, Asr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أتمها افترش رجله اليُسرى، وجلس عليها ناصباً يُمناه. قوله : افترش رجله ؛ بكسر الراء : أي جعله مفروشاً وموضوعاً على الأرض وجلس عليها ؛ لحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يفــرش رجله اليسرى وينصب اليمني ) ، أخرجه مسلم ، وعند النسائي عن ابن عمر الله : «من سنة الصلاة أن تنصب القدم اليمنى، واستقباله بأصابعه إلى القبلة، والجلوس على اليسرى». وفي الباب أخبار وآثار أخر أيضاً بسطها قاسم بن قُطلُوبُغا في رسالته: «الأسوس في كيفية الجلوس»، وبإطلاقها أخذ أصحابنا، فجعلوا هذه الكيفية سنة في جميع جلسات الصلوات. وقد ورد أحاديث مفصلة مصرحة بأنّ جلوسه الا الله على طريق الافتراش كان في القعدة الأولى وغيرها، وفي الأخيرة كان على سبيل التورك (٣) ، أخرجه البخاري وأصحاب السنن وغيرهم، وبه أخذ الشافعي وغيره، بناءً على أن المفصل قاض على المجمل، وهو القول الأعدل. وأما استنان التورّك في الأولى أيضاً كما حكي عن مالك له، فليس له أصل يعتد به، وقد نقحتُ الأمر في «التعليق الممجّد على موطأ محمد»(4) ، وفي «السعاية»(٥).
And when he completes it, he spreads his left foot and sits on it, erecting his right foot. He said: 'He spreads his foot' – with a kasra on the ra (i.e., iftirash) – meaning he makes it spread out and placed on the ground and sits on it; based on the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to spread his left foot and erect his right foot,' narrated by Muslim. And according to al-Nasa'i from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): 'It is from the sunnah of prayer to erect the right foot, face its toes towards the qibla, and sit on the left.' There are other reports and narrations in this chapter detailed by Qasim ibn Qutlubugha in his treatise 'al-Asus fi Kayfiyyat al-Julus'. Based on their general application, our companions (Hanafis) took them and made this manner sunnah in all sittings of the prayers. And detailed hadith have been reported explicitly that his (peace be upon him) sitting in the manner of iftirash was in the first tashahhud and others, and in the last it was in the manner of tawarruk, narrated by al-Bukhari, the authors of Sunan, and others. Al-Shafi'i and others adopted this, based on the principle that the detailed (mufassal) overrides the general (mujmal), and this is the most balanced view. As for the recommendation of tawarruk in the first sitting as well, as narrated from Malik (may Allah have mercy on him), it has no reliable basis. And I have refined the matter in 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad ala Muwatta Muhammad' and in 'al-Si'ayah'.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed inter-madhhab dispute over the sitting posture in prayer. The matn states the Hanafi position: after completing two rak'ahs (or at the end of prayer), one sits in the iftirash position—spreading the left foot and sitting on it, erecting the right foot with toes towards the qibla. The commentator explains that the Hanafi school (ashabuna) has taken the general hadith (like that of Aisha and Ibn Umar) as applying to all sittings, making iftirash sunnah in both the first and final tashahhud. However, detailed hadith (like that of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi) specify that the Prophet used iftirash in the first tashahhud but tawarruk (sitting on the left buttock with legs tucked) in the final one. Imam al-Shafi'i and others adopted this differentiated view, arguing that the specific (mufassal) overrides the general (mujmal). The commentator calls the Shafi'i view 'the most balanced' (al-qawl al-adl) but does not adopt it. He rejects the view of Malik that tawarruk is recommended even in the first sitting as baseless. Thus, the Hanafi position remains iftirash in all sittings, though the Shafi'i position is acknowledged as strong.

Key Terms
افتراش (iftirash): Sitting on the left foot spread flat, with the right foot upright and toes facing the qibla.
تورك (tawarruk): Sitting on the left buttock with both legs tucked to the right, used in the final tashahhud according to Shafi'i.
مجمل (mujmal): A general text that does not specify details; here, the hadith of Aisha.
مفصل (mufassal): A detailed text that specifies particulars; here, the hadith of Abu Humayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the sunnah sitting posture in the final tashahhud?
A) Iftirash
B) Tawarruk
C) Both are equally acceptable
D) It is not sunnah to sit in a specific way

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds iftirash in all sittings, while the Shafi'i school differentiates with tawarruk in the final tashahhud, reflecting a fundamental usul difference in handling general versus specific texts.

Finger and Hand Placement in Tashahhud: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Explains directing fingers towards qibla and hand placement, and the dispute with Shafi'i on clenching fingers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi sunnah for hand and finger placement in tashahhud, explain the Shafi'i position, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between the two schools.

Core Idea
In tashahhud, Hanafis spread all fingers on the thighs; Shafi'is clench the little and ring fingers and point.

The tashahhud is a critical part of the prayer, and its outward form includes specific hand and finger positions. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling based on authentic hadith and the divergence from Imam al-Shafi'i's view. Understanding this difference helps appreciate how the same sunnah can be interpreted differently.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: hands are placed on thighs with all fingers spread out (mabsuta) and directed towards the qibla. This is the sunnah.
The text explicitly says 'mabsuta' (spread).
2
Identify the opposing school and its position
Recognise that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that during tashahhud, the little finger (khinsir) and ring finger (binsir) should be clenched (ya'qid), while the index finger is raised for tawhid.
The text mentions 'wa fihi khilaf al-Shafi'i'.
3
Examine the proofs for each position
The Hanafi proof is based on multiple hadith narrations (Tabarani, Muslim, etc.) showing the Prophet (peace be upon him) placing his hands on his thighs with fingers spread. The Shafi'i proof relies on narrations indicating clenching and pointing.
The source cites extensive chains for the Hanafi view.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is the visible difference in tashahhud: Hanafis spread all fingers, Shafi'is clench the little and ring fingers and raise the index finger. Both are valid within their schools.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

موجهاً أصابعه نحو القبلة، واضعاً يديه على فخذيه موجهاً أصابعه نحو القبلة مبسوطة. قوله : أصابعه ؛ الضمير راجع إلى المصلي، أو إلى الرجل مطلقاً، أو إلى رجله اليُسرى مقيداً ، أو إلى اليمنى بتأويل العضو أو إلى كلِّ واحدٍ من اليُسرى واليمنى، فيفيد كلامه على جميع الاحتمالات إلا الاحتمال الرابع استحباب توجيه أصابع اليسرى المفروشة أيضاً نحو القبلة، وبه صرح في «الكافي». قوله : واضعاً ؛ هو المروي مـن فـعـل رسـول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) في «معجم الطبراني»، و له نن سعيد بن منصور»، و «مصنف عبد الرزاق»، و«جامع الترمذي»، و«السنن» لأبي داود والنسائي وابن خزيمة والبيهقي ومسلم وغيرهم كما بسطه علي القاري المكي في رسالته: «تزيين العبارة بتحسين الإشارة»، والحافظ ابنُ حَجَر في «تلخيص الحبير». وورد عنه في رواية لمسلم وأبي داود والنسائي والترمذي وضع اليدين على الركبتين، وهو محمول على الجواز، والمسنون هو الأوّل كما صرح به في «البحر الرائق». قوله : يعقد الخنصر ؛ بكسر الخاء المعجمة ، وفتح الصاد المهملة، بينهما نون ساكنة : اسم لأصغر أصابع اليد، وما يليه يقال له : بنصر كالخنصر وزناً، وما يليه يقال ... وفيه خلافُ الشَّافِعِي ، فإنَّ عنده يعقدُ الخِنْصَر والبنصر.
Directing his fingers towards the qibla, placing his hands on his thighs, directing his fingers towards the qibla, spread out. He said: 'His fingers' – the pronoun refers to the person praying, or to the foot absolutely, or to his left foot restrictedly, or to the right foot with interpretation of the limb, or to each of the left and right. So his statement benefits on all probabilities except the fourth probability the recommendation of directing the fingers of the spread left foot also towards the qibla, and this is stated in 'al-Kafi'. He said: 'Placing' – it is narrated from the action of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in Mu'jam al-Tabarani, and from Sa'id ibn Mansur, and Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, and Jami' al-Tirmidhi, and Sunan Abi Dawud and al-Nasa'i and Ibn Khuzayma and al-Bayhaqi and Muslim and others, as detailed by Ali al-Qari al-Makki in his treatise 'Tazyin al-Ibara bi Tahsin al-Ishara', and al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar in 'Talkhis al-Habir'. And it is narrated from him in a narration of Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and al-Tirmidhi placing the hands on the knees, and this is carried upon permissibility, and the sunnah is the first as stated in 'al-Bahr al-Raiq'. He said: 'He clenches the little finger' – with a kasra on the khaa and a fatha on the saad, between them a sukun on the noon: it is the name for the smallest finger of the hand, and the one next to it is called 'binsir' similar in weight to 'khinsir', and the one next to it is called ... and in this there is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, for according to him he clenches the little finger and the ring finger.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage details the hand and finger placement during tashahhud. The matn instructs the worshipper to direct his fingers towards the qibla, place his hands on his thighs, and spread his fingers. The commentator explains that the pronoun 'his fingers' could refer to various limbs, but ultimately it establishes the recommendation to direct the fingers of both feet (if sitting) also towards the qibla, as stated in al-Kafi. The sunnah placement of hands is on the thighs, as proven by multiple hadith; placing hands on the knees is also permissible but not the sunnah. Finally, the commentator introduces a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that the little finger (khinsir) and ring finger (binsir) should be clenched (presumably while pointing the index finger), whereas the Hanafi position is to spread all fingers. The text does not complete the Shafi'i description, but it is known that Shafi'is clench the three smallest fingers and point with the index. The Hanafi view is based on hadith describing the Prophet's hands spread on his thighs. The commentator does not explicitly rebut the Shafi'i view but notes the difference.

Key Terms
خنصر (khinsir): The little finger (smallest finger of the hand).
بنصر (binsir): The ring finger (next to the little finger).
مبسوطة (mabsuta): Spread out (fingers not clenched).
يعقد (ya'qid): He clenches (fingers).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the sunnah position of the fingers during tashahhud?
A) Clench the little and ring fingers
B) Point with the index finger only
C) Spread all fingers towards the qibla
D) Place hands on the knees

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sunnah in tashahhud is to spread all fingers on the thighs, while the Shafi'i view clenches the little and ring fingers; both positions are based on authentic hadith and represent legitimate ikhtilaf.

Hand Placement in Tashahhud: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explains Hanafi and Shafi'i hand postures in tashahhud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i hand postures during tashahhud and state the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
The Hanafi sunnah is to spread fingers flat on the thighs; the Shafi'i forms a ring with thumb and middle finger — both based on authentic hadith.

This passage addresses a well-known difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the posture of the hands during the tashahhud sitting. While both schools agree on pointing the index finger to affirm tawhid, they differ on the arrangement of the other fingers and the placement of the hand. The Hanafi school relies on narrations that describe the Prophet (peace be upon him) placing his hands on his thighs with fingers spread toward the qibla. The Shafi'i school follows narrations that depict a ring formation with the thumb and middle finger, bending the little and ring fingers. Understanding this dispute helps clarify the role of different hadith in shaping fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is the Hanafi hand posture? Hands on thighs, fingers spread flat.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
How does Shafi'i differ? Bending little and ring fingers, thumb-middle ring, pointing index.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
What hadith support the Hanafi view? Multiple sources cited, e.g., Wail ibn Hujr from Mu'jam al-Tabarani, Sunan Abu Dawud, etc.
4
Examine the Shafi'i dalil
What hadith support the Shafi'i view? Narrations describing ring formation (not listed in detail but acknowledged).
5
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school reconcile conflicting narrations? The hands-on-knees narration is interpreted as permissible (jawaz), not preferred sunnah.
The Hanafi school does not reject Shafi'i narrations but places them as secondary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله : أصابعه ؛ الضمير راجع إلى المصلي، أو إلى الرجل مطلقاً، أو إلى رجله اليُسرى مقيداً ، أو إلى اليمنى بتأويل العضو أو إلى كلِّ واحدٍ من اليُسرى واليمنى، فيفيد كلامه على جميع الاحتمالات إلا الاحتمال الرابع استحباب توجيه أصابع اليسرى المفروشة أيضاً نحو القبلة، وبه صرح في «الكافي». (2) قوله : واضعاً ؛ هو المروي مـن فـعـل رسـول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) في «معجم الطبراني»، و له نن سعيد بن منصور»، و «مصنف عبد الرزاق»، و«جامع الترمذي»، و«السنن» لأبي داود والنسائي وابن خزيمة والبيهقي ومسلم وغيرهم كما بسطه علي القاري المكي في رسالته: «تزيين العبارة بتحسين الإشارة»، والحافظ ابنُ حَجَر في «تلخيص الحبير». وورد عنه في رواية لمسلم وأبي داود والنسائي والترمذي وضع اليدين على الركبتين، وهو محمول على الجواز، والمسنون هو الأوّل كما صرح به في «البحر الرائق» (3) قوله : يعقد الخنصر ؛ بكسر الخاء المعجمة ، وفتح الصاد المهملة، بينهما نون ساكنة : اسم لأصغر أصابع اليد، وما يليه يقال له : بنصر كالخنصر وزناً، وما يليه يقال
له الوسطى، وما يليه يقال له : المسبحة : لأنه يشار بهما عند التسبيح والتوحيد ، ويقال السبابة أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ العرب كانوا يرفعونها ويشيرون بها إلى خصمه عند سبه وشتمه، والخامسة يقال لها الإبهام بكسر الهمزة. [١]قوله : ويشير؛ ليدلّ على التوحيد، فيكون الفعل موافقاً للقول ، وقد ثبتت الإشارة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بروايات كثيرة أكثرها صريحة صحيحة، لا مرة لها، مخرجة في الكتب الشهيرة ؛ كالسنن الأربعة، وصحيح مسلم» و«سنن البيهقي و «مسند أحمد»، و«موطأ مالك»، و«شرح معاني الآثار للطحاوي، و«معاجم الطبراني» و سنن سعيد بن منصور»، و «مصنف عبد الرزاق» و«ابن أبي شيبة» وغيرها، كما بسطه علي القاري في رسالته: تزيين العبارة»، ورسالته الأخرى «التدهين للتزيين». [٢] قوله : ومثل هذا ؛ أي الكيفية المذكورة عن الشافعي مع الإشارة بالسبابة إنما قال : مثل ؛ لأنّ أصحابنا وإن قالوا بالإشارة كالشافعية وغيرهم، إلا أنَّ بيننا وبينهم
Directing his fingers toward the qibla, placing his hands on his thighs, directing his fingers toward the qibla, spread out. In this there is a dispute with al-Shafi'i, for he holds that one forms a ring with the little finger and ring finger. [1] His saying: 'his fingers' – the pronoun refers to the worshipper, or to the foot absolutely, or specifically to the left foot, or to the right foot by interpretation of 'limb', or to each of the left and right. Thus his speech, on all probabilities except the fourth, indicates the recommendation of directing the fingers of the left foot also toward the qibla when it is spread, as stated in al-Kafi. (2) His saying: 'placing' – this is reported from the action of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in Mu'jam al-Tabarani, [the narration of] Ibn Sa'id ibn Mansur, Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, Jami' al-Tirmidhi, and the Sunan of Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Khuzayma, al-Bayhaqi, Muslim, and others as expanded upon by Ali al-Qari in his treatise 'Tazyin al-'Ibara bi Tahsin al-Ishara', and al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar in Talkhis al-Habir. Also reported from him in a narration of Muslim, Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and al-Tirmidhi is the placement of the hands on the knees, and this is interpreted as permissible, while the sunnah is the first, as stated in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. (3) His saying: 'forms a ring with the little finger' – with kasra on the kha' and fatha on the sad, between them a sukun on the nun: it is the name for the smallest finger of the hand. The one next to it is called 'ring finger' like 'little finger' in weight. The one next to it is called 'middle finger'. The one next to it is called 'al-musabbiha' because it is used when glorifying and affirming God's oneness. It is also called 'al-sabbaba' because the Arabs used to raise it and point at an opponent when insulting and reviling him. The fifth is called 'thumb' with kasra on the hamza. [1] His saying: 'and he points' – to indicate tawhid, so that the action agrees with the statement; and the pointing is established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) by many narrations, most of which are explicit and authentic, without any doubt, found in well-known books like the four Sunan, Sahih Muslim, Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Musnad Ahmad, Muwatta' Malik, Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar by al-Tahawi, the Mu'jams of al-Tabarani, Sunan Sa'id ibn Mansur, Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, and others, as expanded by Ali al-Qari in his treatise 'Tazyin al-'Ibara' and his other treatise 'al-Tadhyeen li al-Tazyin'. [2] His saying: 'and similar to this' – meaning the manner mentioned about al-Shafi'i with the pointing of the index finger. He only said 'similar' because our companions, although they say pointing like the Shafi'is and others, yet between us and them [there is a difference].
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the placement of hands and fingers during the tashahhud sitting in prayer. The Hanafi position is that the hands are placed on the thighs with the fingers spread out and directed toward the qibla. The text explicitly states that this is the reported sunnah from multiple hadith collections. The Shafi'i position, as mentioned, is to form a 'ring' (عقد) by bending the little finger and ring finger, then making a circle with the thumb and middle finger, and pointing the index finger (السبابة) when uttering the two shahadahs. The Hanafi commentary then clarifies that the pronoun 'his fingers' could refer to various body parts, but the general meaning is that both the left foot and the fingers of both feet should be directed toward the qibla. The commentary also notes that there is a narration about placing hands on the knees, but this is interpreted as permissible, not the preferred sunnah. The second part of the passage discusses the index finger gesture. Both schools agree on raising and pointing the index finger, but the commentator notes that there is a difference between the Hanafis and Shafi'is in the overall hand posture (the Hanafis keep the hand flat and spread on the thigh, while the Shafi'is make a ring). The commentator emphasizes that the pointing is established by many authentic hadiths. The passage thus preserves the Hanafi view as the sunnah while acknowledging the Shafi'i view and providing evidence for both practices.

Key Terms
الخنصر (al-khinsir): The smallest finger of the hand; the little finger.
البنصر (al-binsir): The ring finger; the finger next to the little finger.
السبابة (al-sabbaba): The index finger; used for pointing during the tashahhud to indicate tawhid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Hanafis do not point the index finger; Shafi'is do point it.
D) Hanafis make a ring with thumb and middle finger; Shafi'is spread all fingers flat.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi hand posture in tashahhud is to spread fingers flat on the thighs and point the index finger, in contrast to the Shafi'i ring formation; both are grounded in authentic hadith.

The Two Hanafi Views on Finger Placement in Tashahhud
Intra-madhhab dispute on finger placement in tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on hand placement during tashahhud and identify which is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has two valid sayings: spread without pointing (mashhur) vs. spread then ring and point (mufta bihi).

This lesson explores the intra-madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the recommended finger position during tashahhud. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that only two positions are valid, and we will learn their description and authoritative basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Hanafi views
Read the passage to find the two sayings: first, spreading the fingers without any pointing; second, spreading initially then forming a ring (tahliq/aqd) and pointing at the shahada.
The second view is the mufta bihi.
2
Locate the key terms for each view
Understand the terms 'bast' (spreading), 'tahliq' (ring formation), and 'isharah' (pointing) as they appear in the text.
These terms are defined in the key terms section.
3
Identify the deviant third view
The text mentions a third view (pointing without ring) and dismisses it as shad (deviant). Recognize why it is rejected.
Only two views are considered sound within the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة صفة الصلاة
فرقاً من حيث أن المسنونَ عند الشافعي له عدم بسط اليمنى، والتحليق من ابتداء الجلوس على ما هو ظاهر أكثر الأحاديث.
والمختار عند أصحابنا على ما صرح به علي القاري وغيره هو البسط من الابتداء، ثم التحليق والإشارة عند الشهادتين، واختار بعض المتأخرين من أصحابنا الإشارة بدون التحليق مطلقاً، وهو قول شادٌ لا يعرّج عليه، كما بسطه في «رد المحتار على الدر المختار»، ولإيراد لفظ : «المثل»، توجيهات أخر أيضاً بسطناها في «السعاية».
(1) ينظر: «المنهاج» (1 : 173)، قال النووي في كيفيته ويقبض من يمناه الخنصر والبنصر، وكذا الوسطى في الأظهر، ويرسل المسبحة ويرفعها عند قوله : إلا الله، ولا يحركها، والأظهر ضمّ الإبهام إليها كعاقد ثلاثة وخمسين. (2) لكن هذا القول وهو الإشارة مع البسط بدون العقد صححه صاحب «المواهب»(ق26/أ) ، و«المراقي» (ص 270 - (271)، و«تحفة الملوك ( ص) (75) ، والدر المختار (1: 341 - 342) ، و(الدر المنتقى (1 : 100 ). (3) قال ابن عابدين في رد المحتار » (1) : 509) : (فهذه النقول كلها صريحة بأن الإشارة المسنونة إنما على كيفية خاصة وهي العقد أو التحليق، وأما رواية بسط الأصابع فليس فيها إشارة أصلاً، ولهذا قال في «الفتح» و «شرح المنية : وهذا أي ما ذكر من الكيفية فرع تصحيح الإشارة: أي مفرع على تصحيح رواية الإشارة ، فليس لنا قول بالإشارة بدون تحليق. ولهذا فسرت الإشارة بهذه الكيفية في عامة الكتب كـ«البدائع» و«النهاية» و«معراج الدراية» و«الذخيرة» و«الظهيرية» و«فتح القدير» وشرحي المنية» و«القُهستاني» و«الحلبة» و«النهر» و«شرح الملتقى» للبهنسي معزيا إلى شرح النقاية وشرحي «درر البحار» وغيرها كما ذكرت عباراتهم في رسالة سميتها رفع التردد في عقد الأصابع عند التشهد» وحررت فيها أنه ليس لنا سوى قولين :
الأول : وهو المشهور في المذهب بسط الأصابع بدون إشارة. الثاني : بسط الأصابع إلى حين الشهادة، فيعقد عندها ويرفع السبابة عند النفي ويضعها عند الإثبات، وهذا ما اعتمده المتأخرون لثبوته عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بالأحاديث الصحيحة ولصحة نقله عن أئمتنا الثلاثة.

جاءَ عن علمائنا أيضاً .
1 ] قوله : عن علمائنا ؛ أي عن أئمتنا ومشايخنا ، فقد روى الإمام محمد في «الموطأ» بسنده عن ابن عمر : «كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا جلس وضع كفه اليمنى على فخذه اليمنى، وقبض أصابعه كلها ، وأشار بأصبعه التي تلي الإبهام، ووضع كفه اليسرى على فخذه اليسرى ، ثم قال : قال محمد وهو قول أبي حنيفة نأخذ وبصنع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم . انتهى"
وذكر ابن الهمام وغيره من شراح «الهداية»: «إن أبا يوسف ذكر في «الأمالي»: إنه يعقد الخنصر والبنصر، ويُحلق بالوسطى والإبهام، ويشير بالسبابة». انتهى.
وهذا أي استنانُ الإشارة بالسبابة مع الكيفية المذكورة قد صححه واعتمد عليه كثير من أصحابنا، كما لا يخفى على من طالع نوازل الفقيه أبي الليث»، و«الذخيرة» و«الغُنية» و«الحلبة»، و«فتح القدير»، و«البحر»، و«النهر»، و«الخانية»، و«المجتبى شرح مختصر القدوري» و«الدر المختار وحواشيه، و«مواهب الرحمن» وشرحه «البرهان»، و«المحيط» و «شروح مجمع البحرين» و «مراقي الفلاح» و«درر البحار وشرحه غرر الأفكار وتزيين «العبارة» و«التحفة» و«البناية»، وغيرها.
فلذا قال في «الفتح»: إن الأول خلاف الدراية والرواية. وأما ما عليه عامة الناس في زماننا من الإشارة مع البسط بدون عقد فلم أر أحداً قال به سوى الشارح تبعاً للشرنبلالي عن البرهان للعلامة إبراهيم الطرابلسي صاحب «الإسعاف» من أهل القرن العاشر. وإذا عارض كلامه كلام جمهور الشارحين من المتقدمين والمتأخرين من ذكر القولين فقط فالعمل على ما عليه جمهور العلماء لا جمهور العوام ، فأخرج نفسك من ظلمة التقليد وحيرة الأوهام، واستضئ بمصباح التحقيق في هذا المقام فإنه من منح الملك العلام.
Chapter of Prayer, Description of Prayer. A difference: The sunnah according to al-Shafi'i is not to spread the right hand, but to form a ring from the beginning of sitting, as is apparent from most hadiths. The chosen (mukhtar) view of our companions, as stated by Ali al-Qari and others, is to spread from the beginning, then to form a ring and point at the two testimonies (shahadatayn). Some later scholars among us chose pointing without forming a ring absolutely, and it is a deviant saying not to be relied upon, as expanded in Radd al-Muhtar ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar. And for the word 'mithl', there are other explanations which we expanded in al-Si'aya. (1) See: al-Minhaj (1:173), al-Nawawi said about its manner: He grips from his right hand the little finger and ring finger, and likewise the middle finger in the apparent view, and lets go of the index finger and raises it when saying 'illa Allah', and does not move it, and the apparent view is to join the thumb to it like one who knots three and fifty-three. (2) But this saying, which is pointing with spread without knotting, was declared sahih by the author of al-Mawahib (q26/a), and al-Maraqi (p. 270-271), and Tuhfat al-Muluk (p. 75), and al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:341-342), and al-Durr al-Muntaqa (1:100). (3) Ibn Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar (1:509): 'All these transmitted texts are explicit that the pointed finger recommended (masnun) is only in a specific manner, which is knotting (al-aqd) or forming a ring (al-tahliq). As for the narration of spreading the fingers, there is no pointing in it at all. For this reason, it was said in al-Fath and Sharh al-Munyah: 'This, i.e., what was mentioned of the manner, is a branch of authenticating the pointing: i.e., it is based on authenticating the narration of pointing. So we have no saying of pointing without forming a ring. For this reason, pointing is interpreted with this manner in most books, such as al-Bada'i', al-Nihayah, Mi'raj al-Dirayah, al-Dhakhirah, al-Zuhayriyyah, Fath al-Qadir, the two commentaries of al-Munyah, al-Quhistani, al-Halabah, al-Nahr, Sharh al-Multaqa of al-Bahnasi attributed to Sharh al-Niqayah, the two commentaries of Durar al-Bihar, etc., as I mentioned their statements in a treatise I named Raf' al-Taraddud fi 'Aqd al-Asabi' ind al-Tashahhud, and I concluded therein that we have only two sayings: First: the well-known (mashhur) in the madhhab: spreading the fingers without pointing. Second: spreading the fingers until the time of testimony (shahada), then knots at that point, raises the index finger at the negation and puts it down at the affirmation. This is what the later scholars relied upon due to its being confirmed from the Prophet (peace be upon him) by authentic hadiths, and due to its authentic transmission from our three Imams.' It also came from our scholars. [1] His statement: 'from our scholars' means from our imams and shaykhs. Imam Muhammad narrated in al-Muwatta' with his chain from Ibn Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to sit, placing his right palm on his right thigh, clenching all his fingers, and pointing with the finger next to the thumb, and placing his left palm on his left thigh.' Then he (Muhammad) said: 'This is the saying of Abu Hanifa. We take it and we act by what the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did.' End. Ibn al-Humam and others from the commentators of al-Hidayah mentioned: 'Abu Yusuf mentioned in al-Amali that he knots the little finger and ring finger, forms a ring with the middle finger and thumb, and points with the index finger.' End. And this, i.e., the recommendation of pointing with the index finger with the aforementioned manner, was declared sahih and relied upon by many of our companions, as is not hidden from one who peruses the Nawazil of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, al-Dhakhirah, al-Ghunyah, al-Halabah, Fath al-Qadir, al-Bahr, al-Nahr, al-Khaniyyah, al-Mujtaba Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses, Mawahib al-Rahman and its commentary al-Burhan, al-Muhit, the commentaries of Majma' al-Bahrayn, Muraqi al-Falah, Durar al-Bihar and its commentary Ghurar al-Afkar, Tajwiz al-Ibarah, al-Tuhfah, al-Binayah, etc. Therefore, it was said in al-Fath: 'The first (view) is contrary to dirayah and riwayah.' As for what the general people in our time do, i.e., pointing with spread (fingers) without knotting, I have not seen anyone who said it except the commentator (al-Sharh) following al-Shurunbulali from al-Burhan of the scholar Ibrahim al-Tarabulsi, the author of al-Is'af from the people of the tenth century. When his statement contradicts the statement of the majority of commentators (sharihin) from the earlier and later ones who only mention two sayings, then action is upon what the majority of scholars are upon, not the majority of commoners. So take yourself out of the darkness of taqlid and the confusion of fancies, and seek illumination with the lamp of investigation in this station, for it is among the gifts of the King, the All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a detailed discussion on the recommended hand and finger placement during the tashahhud in prayer, specifically addressing the Hanafi school's position and the disputes within it. The passage begins by contrasting the Shafi'i position (tahliq from the beginning) with the Hanafi mukhtar (chosen) view: spreading the fingers initially, then forming a ring (tahliq) and pointing the index finger at the shahada. Some later Hanafis adopted pointing without the ring, but this is dismissed as a deviant (shad) opinion. A long quote from Ibn Abidin's Radd al-Muhtar is then given, which clarifies that Hanafi has only two valid positions: (1) spreading without pointing (the mashhur) and (2) spreading until the shahada, then forming a ring and pointing (relied upon by later scholars due to authentic hadiths). The passage then provides evidence from Imam Muhammad's Muwatta' via Ibn Umar, showing the Prophet's practice of clenching the fingers and pointing. It also cites Abu Yusuf's view from al-Amali, which involves knotting the little and ring fingers and forming a ring with middle finger and thumb. The author then lists numerous Hanafi authorities who have adopted and strengthened the latter position. Finally, the author criticizes the common practice of pointing without the ring formation, attributing it to a minority view from al-Shurunbulali and al-Tarabulsi, and urges following the majority of scholars, not the common people.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-isharah): Pointing the index finger during tashahhud, specifically at the shahada.
التحليق (al-tahliq): Forming a circle by joining the thumb and middle finger.
العقد (al-aqd): Knotting the fingers; here specifically clenching little and ring finger while forming ring with middle and thumb.
البسط (al-bast): Spreading the fingers of the right hand without clutching or pointing.
المختار (al-mukhtar): The chosen/preferred view within the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two valid views on tashahhud finger placement; the mufta bihi is to spread then form a ring and point at the shahada.

The Sunnah of Pointing the Finger in Tashahhud: Refuting the Opposition
Critique of scholars who ruled against the sunnah of ishara

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pointing the finger in tashahhud, identify the proof from hadith, and explain why moving the finger is not sunnah.

Core Idea
Pointing the finger in tashahhud is a confirmed sunnah; moving it is a rejected anomaly.

The act of pointing the index finger (ishara) during tashahhud is a well-known practice in the Hanafi school, but some later scholars disputed its sunnah status. In this passage, the commentator refutes those opinions with strong language, citing authentic hadith and the positions of the founding imams. You will learn the relied-upon ruling and the critical distinction between pointing and moving the finger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in dispute
The passage addresses two acts: pointing (ishara) and moving (tahrik) the index finger during tashahhud. The first is defended as sunnah; the second is rejected as shadh.
2
Locate the mainstream Hanafi ruling
The author affirms that pointing is sunnah muakkadah, citing numerous Hanafi authorities. Some later scholars ruled it disliked, but the author refutes them.
The footnote lists many authorities who prefer spreading the fingers without pointing.
3
Examine the proof
The primary dalil is the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr, which mentions 'raising his finger' (raf'a isba'ahu) — this establishes the act of pointing.
The hadith also includes 'moving it' but that part is anomalous.
4
Evaluate the anomaly (shudhudh)
Hadith critics note that eleven trustworthy narrators omitted the word 'moving it'; only Za'ida includes it. Hence, the movement is considered shadh and not part of the sunnah.
The author lists the eleven narrators to demonstrate the weight of evidence.
5
Draw the conclusion
Pointing (without movement) is sunnah muakkadah and the fatwa is upon it. Moving the finger is not sunnah and is condemned by the commentator.
The Maliki scholar Ibn al-'Arabi is quoted to reinforce the rejection of moving.

And it is a great wonder from some of our scholars, such as the author of al-Zahira, al-Khulasa, al-Inaya, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Tatarkhaniyya, Jami' al-Mudammarat, and others: they gave fatwa that the pointing (al-ishara) is not sunnah, rather it is disliked. And al-Kaydani added in his Khulasa: a tune in the tanbur, and he counted it among the prohibited things, while there is no evidence with them for what they mentioned, no support for them, neither in narration nor in reason. And it, while contradicting the authentic explicit hadiths, also contradicts our imams. In summary, following the scholars who gave fatwa of dislike contradicts the action of our Prophet, peace be upon him, and the sayings of our Imam and his students, especially after the truth is clear and honesty is evident; it is not befitting for a Muslim, let alone a scholar. So let one be mindful. (2) (1) As for spreading the fingers without pointing, it is the choice of the author (al-Marghinani), al-Tahawi in his Mukhtasar, al-Quduri in his Mukhtasar, the author of al-Hidaya, al-Kanz, al-Multaqa, al-Mukhtar, al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya, Ghurar al-Ahkam, Khulasat al-Kaydani, its commentary by al-Rayhani, and in al-Tanwir: and upon it is the fatwa. (2) And let one also be mindful that the relied-upon fiqh schools did not say to move the index finger, because of the narration of Za'ida from 'Asim al-Jarmi from his father from Wa'il ibn Hujr who said: 'He sat, spread his left leg, placed his left palm on his left thigh and knee, placed the edge of his right elbow on his right thigh, then he grasped two of his fingers and made a ring, then raised his finger and I saw him moving it, supplicating with it' in Sahih Ibn Khuzayma, Sahih Ibn Hibban, Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra, al-Mujtaba, Musnad Ahmad, and others; because the hadith critics considered the word 'moving it' as shadh. Al-Hafiz Ibn Khuzayma said in his Sahih: 'In none of the reports is 'moving it' except in this report of Za'ida.' And in Tadhkirat al-'Abd al-Awwah: 'After tracking the hadith in the Musnad, Sunan, Mu'jams, and others, we found that eleven trustworthy hadith critics all narrated the hadith of our master Wa'il, and they did not mention the word of moving, and only Za'ida, who is trustworthy, narrated the movement, and this is undoubtedly shudhudh, especially since there is a sound report explicitly stating no movement, namely the report of Ibn al-Zubayr which the critics authenticated, and the report of our master Ibn 'Umar in Sahih Muslim, with no mention of movement at all. Let us list the names of the trustworthy critics who narrated the hadith of Wa'il without mentioning movement, and those who opposed them, Za'ida, who added movement: Sufyan al-Thawri, Sufyan ibn 'Uyayna, Shu'ba ibn al-Hajjaj, 'Abd al-Wahid ibn Ziyad, 'Abd Allah ibn Idris, Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiya, Abu 'Awana al-Yashkuri, Abu al-Ahwas Sallam ibn Sulaym, Bishr ibn al-Mufaddal, Khalid ibn 'Abd Allah al-Tahhan, and all these are trustworthy critics, and Ghaylan ibn Jami' who is trustworthy... This definitively proves that the movement is shadh.' And al-Hafiz Ibn al-'Arabi al-Maliki said in Tuhfat al-Ahwadhi: 'Beware of moving your fingers during tashahhud, and do not pay attention to the narration of al-'Utbiyya, for it is a calamity. It is amazing that he says: it is a scourge for the devil when moved. Know that if you move a finger for the devil, he will move ten for you. The devil is only repelled by sincerity, humility, dhikr, and seeking refuge, not by moving it.' (1) Al-Si'aya (1:223) and onwards. (2) Al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad (1:464-465) and onwards.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from the commentator, critiquing a group of Hanafi scholars who issued fatwas that pointing the index finger (al-ishara) during tashahhud is not sunnah, or even disliked (makruh). The author expresses astonishment at these rulings, stating they contradict authentic hadith and the positions of the founding Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa and his students). He specifically mentions al-Kaydani, who went further by deeming the act haram, likening it to a 'tune in a tanbur' (i.e., an innovation). The author argues that these scholars have no evidence (dalil) from narration (riwaya) or reason (diraya). He warns that following such opinions is unbecoming for a Muslim, especially a scholar, after the truth is clear. The first footnote clarifies that spreading the fingers without pointing is the chosen position (muhtar) of many Hanafi authorities, including the author of al-Hidaya, and that the fatwa is upon this view. The second footnote addresses the issue of moving the finger (tahrik). It cites the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr, which includes the phrase 'moving it,' but explains that hadith critics (huffaz) consider this wording as shadh (anomalous). It lists eleven trustworthy narrators who omitted this phrase, establishing that moving the finger is not part of the sunnah. The commentary also quotes al-Hafiz Ibn al-'Arabi al-Maliki, who strongly condemns moving the finger, arguing it is ineffective against the devil and may even empower him. Thus, the passage upholds the sunnah of pointing (without movement) and refutes both the denial of pointing and the practice of moving.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-ishara): Pointing the index finger during tashahhud; the act of raising and pointing the finger as part of the prayer.
التحريك (al-tahrik): Moving the index finger back and forth during tashahhud; considered shadh (anomalous) in this passage.
شاذ (shadh): A hadith that contradicts a more reliable narration; here, the addition of 'moving' in Za'ida's narration is considered shadh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is makruh
C) It is shadh and not part of the sunnah
D) It is wajib

Key Takeaway
Pointing the index finger (ishara) in tashahhud is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah muakkadah) in the Hanafi school; moving it (tahrik) is an anomalous innovation (shadh) that contradicts the stronger hadith tradition.

The Tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud: Text and Ruling
Stating the tashahhud text and its ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the full text of the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud and explain its Hanafi ruling (wajib in the final sitting, sunnah in the first sitting).

Core Idea
The tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is the authentic recitation for the sitting in prayer; it is wajib in the final sitting.

This passage from Al-Wiqayah specifies the precise wording of the tashahhud to be recited during the sitting in prayer. It emphasizes that the tashahhud taught by the Prophet to Ibn Mas'ud is the most authentic and widely accepted version. Understanding this text and its legal status is essential for performing the prayer correctly according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the tashahhud text
Learn the Arabic text of the tashahhud as narrated by Ibn Mas'ud word for word.
The text begins: 'التَّحِيَّاتُ لِلَّهِ وَالصَّلَوَاتُ وَالطَّيِّبَاتُ...'
2
Understand the meaning of each phrase
Translate each phrase into your language to grasp its spiritual and legal significance.
For example, 'التحيات لله' means 'All greetings belong to Allah.'
3
Recognize the source and authenticity
Note that this tashahhud is narrated by Ibn Mas'ud and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi and the six major hadith collectors.
Al-Tirmidhi: 'It is the most authentic hadith narrated from the Prophet concerning the tashahhud.'
4
Learn the Hanafi ruling
Understand that reciting this tashahhud in the final sitting is wajib; in the first sitting (if applicable) it is sunnah.
Omission of a wajib requires sujud al-sahw, but does not invalidate the prayer.
5
Distinguish fard vs. wajib
In Hanafi, a wajib is proven by a zanni dalil; its omission requires compensation, not repetition.
Compare with Shafi'i school which considers tashahhud as fard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a matn (text) from Al-Wiqayah, stating the recommended tashahhud to recite during the sitting position in prayer. The commentary explains that the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is the one intended, as taught by the Prophet to Ibn Mas'ud. The full text of the tashahhud is provided: it begins with 'التَّحِيَّاتُ لِلَّهِ وَالصَّلَوَاتُ وَالطَّيِّبَاتُ' and includes the greetings upon the Prophet and the attestation of faith. The commentator notes that this hadith is narrated by the six major hadith collectors (al-Kutub al-Sittah) and is considered the most authentic regarding the tashahhud, as stated by Imam al-Tirmidhi. The commentator also mentions that other forms of tashahhud exist, which he has discussed elsewhere. In the Hanafi school, reciting this tashahhud is wajib (obligatory) in the final sitting, and it is a sunnah in the first sitting (if applicable). The text emphasizes the importance of adhering to the authentic narration.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of greetings and attestation of faith during the sitting position in prayer; the specific text attributed to Ibn Mas'ud.
التحيات (al-tahiyyat): The opening word of the tashahhud, meaning 'greetings'; it is a fixed formula.

Key Terms from Source Passage
