The Tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud and the Ruling on Finger Movement
Establishes the wording of tashahhud and disproves finger movement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the tashahhud wording and finger movement, and evaluate the proof for each based on hadith authenticity.

Core Idea
The tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is authentic; finger movement during tashahhud is not a sunnah and is discouraged.

The tashahhud is a vital part of the prayer, but its exact wording and accompanying actions differ among madhhabs. This lesson focuses on the Hanafi position: we recite the tashahhud exactly as the Prophet taught Abd Allah ibn Mas'ud, and we do not move the index finger. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the evidence for both rulings, relying on hadith criticism to settle the dispute about finger movement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
The passage presents two distinct fiqh rulings: (1) the preferred wording of tashahhud is that of Ibn Mas'ud, and (2) moving the index finger during tashahhud is not a sunnah and is discouraged. Understand that these are separate issues within the same ritual act.
The first ruling is about the text recited; the second is about a physical gesture.
2
Locate the dalil for the tashahhud wording
The proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud narrated in the six canonical collections. The Prophet taught him to say 'At-tahiyyatu lillahi...' At-Tirmidhi declared it the most authentic hadith on tashahhud. This establishes the wording as the sunnah.
The dalil is ahad, but its authenticity is strong enough to act upon.
3
Analyze the dalil against finger movement
The passage lists trustworthy narrators who transmitted the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr without mentioning finger movement. Only Za'idah added the movement. The commentator argues this addition is shadhdh (anomalous) and therefore not reliable.
The argument is based on hadith criticism – the majority of reliable narrators outweigh the lone addition.
4
Note the inter-madhhab ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school considers moving the finger a sunnah based on the same hadith of Wa'il. The Hanafi position rejects this because the movement is not affirmed by the majority of narrators. Recognize this as a legitimate difference of ijtihad.
The Hanafi tarjih prioritizes the more widespread narration.
5
Apply the ruling to practice
In practice, a Hanafi recites the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud and does not move the index finger. If one sees others moving their finger, they should follow the stronger evidence.
The mufta bihi is to remain still; moving is makruh tanzih.

ويتشهد كابن مسعود

هناك رواية صحيحة مصرحة بعدم التحريك، وهو رواية ابن الزبير التي صححها الحفاظ، ورواية سيدنا ابن عمر له في (صحيح مسلم»، وليس فيها ذكر للتحريك مطلقاً، ولنسرد أسماء الثقات الحفاظ الذي رووا حديث وائل دون ذكر التحريك ، والذي خالفهم زائدة الذي زاد فيه التحريك : سفيان الثوري وسفيان بن عيينه ، وشعبة بن الحجاج، وعبد الواحد بن زياد، وعبد الله بن إدريس، وزهير بن معاوية، وأبو عوانة اليشكري، وأبو الأحوص سلام بن سليم، وبشر بن المفضل وخالد بن عبد الله الطحان وكل هؤلاء ثقات حفاظ، وغيلان بن جامع وهو ثقة... وهذا يثبت قطعاً أن التحريك شاذ». وقال الحافظ ابن العربي المالكي في تحفة الأحوذي (۲): ٨٥) : «وإياكم وتحريك أصابعكم في التشهد، ولا تلتفتوا إلى رواية العتبيّة فإنها بلية، وعجباً ما يقول : إنها مقمعة للشيطان إذا حركت، واعلموا أنكم إذا حركتم للشيطان أصبعاً حرك لكم عشراً، إنما يقمع الشيطان بالإخلاص والخشوع والذكر والاستعاذة فأما بتحريكه فلا».
And he performs the tashahhud like Ibn Mas'ud. [1] His statement: 'And he performs the tashahhud like Ibn Mas'ud,' meaning like the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud, or as Ibn Mas'ud narrated from the Prophet that he taught him to say in the sitting: 'Greetings, prayers, and good deeds are for Allah. Peace be upon you, O Prophet, and the mercy of Allah and His blessings. Peace be upon us and upon the righteous servants of Allah. I bear witness that there is no god but Allah, and I bear witness that Muhammad is His servant and Messenger.' The six major hadith collectors reported his hadith. At-Tirmidhi said: 'It is the most authentic hadith reported about the tashahhud, and the majority of scholars from the Companions and after them act upon it.' Other tashahhuds have also been reported, as I detailed in at-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad. (0) There is a authentic narration explicitly stating not to move (the finger), and it is the narration of Ibn az-Zubayr which the hadith masters authenticated, and the narration of our master Ibn 'Umar in Sahih Muslim, and in it there is no mention of moving at all. Let us list the names of trustworthy hadith masters who narrated the hadith of Wa'il without mentioning moving, and those who opposed them is Za'idah who added moving: Sufyan ath-Thawri, Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah, Shu'bah ibn al-Hajjaj, 'Abd al-Wahid ibn Ziyad, 'Abdullah ibn Idris, Zuhayr ibn Mu'awiyah, Abu 'Awanah al-Yashkuri, Abu al-Ahwas Sallam ibn Sulaym, Bishr ibn al-Mufaddal, Khalid ibn 'Abdullah at-Tahhan - all these are trustworthy memorizers, and Ghaylan ibn Jami' who is trustworthy... This definitively proves that the movement is anomalous.' Al-Hafiz Ibn al-'Arabi al-Maliki said in Tuhfat al-Ahwadhi (2:85): 'Beware of moving your fingers in the tashahhud, and pay no attention to the narration of al-'Utbiyyah for it is a calamity. And it is surprising that he says it is a means to repel the devil when moved. Know that if you move a finger for the devil, he will move ten for you. The devil is only repelled by sincerity, humility, dhikr, and seeking refuge, not by moving.'
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'ويتشهد كابن مسعود' (And he performs the tashahhud like Ibn Mas'ud). The author explains that the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is the one to be recited, quoting the famous hadith from the six canonical collections. He then cites at-Tirmidhi's authentication of this tashahhud. The footnote (marked with (0) and then a long quotation) addresses the issue of moving the index finger during tashahhud. The author argues that the most authentic narrations of the Prophet's tashahhud (including the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr) do not mention finger movement. He lists several trustworthy narrators who transmitted the hadith without mention of movement, while only Za'idah added it, making that addition anomalous (shadhdh). He then quotes Ibn al-'Arabi al-Maliki who strongly discourages finger movement, stating that it is not a sunnah and that the devil is not repelled by physical gestures but by sincerity and humility. The Hanafi school follows the position of not moving the finger during tashahhud, based on the preponderance of evidence.

Key Terms
تشهد (tashahhud): The testimony recited in the sitting of prayer, consisting of greetings and the shahada.
تحريك (tahrik): Moving the index finger during tashahhud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it is the shortest wording.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school follows the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud without moving the finger, based on the preponderance of authentic narrations.

Ruling on First Sitting and Recitation in Last Rak'ahs
States rulings for the first sitting and recitation in the last rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in the last two rak'ahs and explain the evidence from hadith and companion practice.

Core Idea
Reciting al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs is sunnah; omitting it is permissible and does not invalidate prayer.

The first sitting (qa'dah ula) and the recitation in the last two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer have specific rulings in the Hanafi school. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains that only tashahhud is recited in the first sitting, and in the last two rak'ahs reciting only al-Fatiha is the sunnah, but one may also remain silent or glorify Allah. The ruling is supported by authentic hadith and the practice of the Companions, and the Shafi'i school holds a different view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage covers two acts: the first sitting (middle tashahhud) and recitation in the last two rak'ahs. For the first sitting, the ruling is that nothing is added to the tashahhud (no salawat or du'a). For the last two rak'ahs, reciting only al-Fatiha is sunnah (better), and omitting recitation or doing tasbih is permissible (ja'iz).
The term 'first sitting' refers to the sitting after the second rak'ah in a 4-rak'ah prayer.
2
Locate the dalil for each ruling
For the first sitting, the dalil is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud where the Prophet taught the tashahhud and indicated that in the middle sitting one stands after tashahhud, while in the final sitting one may supplicate. For the last two rak'ahs, the primary dalil is the hadith of Abu Qatada that the Prophet recited only al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs of Dhuhr and 'Asr.
The hadith of Abu Qatada is reported by all six major hadith compilers except Tirmidhi.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the dalil proves the ruling)
The Prophet's actual practice (fi'l) of reciting only al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs indicates that this is the sunnah. The permissibility of omitting recitation is derived from the practice of Ibn Mas'ud and the report from 'Ali giving an option between reciting and not reciting. These show that recitation is not obligatory.
In Hanafi usul, the Prophet's action (fi'l) generally indicates sunnah, not obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement) with al-Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that recitation in every rak'ah is obligatory and that the final sitting posture should be different (tawarruk). The Hanafi school differs, relying on the explicit hadith and companion practice.
The phrase 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' in the matn signals the ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred position)
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) because it is based on authentic ahadith and the practice of the Companions. The ruling gives flexibility: one may recite only al-Fatiha (sunnah) or omit recitation entirely (permissible), and the prayer remains valid without any compensation.
The authority weight is 'zahir riwayah' as it comes from the established narrations.

ولا يزيد عليه في القعدة الأولى، ويقرأ فيما بعد الأوليين الفاتحة فقط، وهي أفضل، وإن سبح أو سكت جاز، ويقعد كالأولى خلافاً للشافعي
[١]قوله : ولا يزيد ؛ أي فلا يصلي فيه على النبي ، ولا يدعو ؛ لرواية أحمد عن ابن مسعود : «علمني رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم التشهد في أول الصلاة وآخرها، فإذا كان وسط الصلاة نهض إذا فرغ من التشهد ، وإذا كان في آخر الصلاة دعا لنفسه ما شاء». (٢) قوله : فقط ؛ أي لا يضم السورة ، لما أخرجه السنّة إلا الترمذي عن أبي قتادة ه: «كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقرأ في الأوليين من الظهر والعصر بفاتحة الكتاب وسورتين، وفي الأخْريّين بفاتحة الكتاب» (۳) ، وهذا هو السنّة كما في «الموطأ»، فإن زاد على الفاتحة فيهما فلا بأس به ولا تجب سجدتا السهو على الأصح ، كما في «الغُنية». [۳] قوله : جاز ؛ لما روى محمد له في «الموطأ» : «إن ابن مسعود ه كان لا يقرأ في الأخريين شيئاً)، وعن علي له أيضاً «روي التخيير بين القراءة وعدمها في الأخريين»(0) أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، وهذا على سبيل الإجزاء والكفاية، والمسنون هو القراءة في الأُخْرَيَين.
And he does not increase (anything) upon it in the first sitting, and he recites in what is after the first two (rak'ahs) only al-Fatiha, and it is better, and if he glorifies (Allah) or remains silent, it is permissible. And he sits like the first (sitting), contrary to al-Shafi'i. [1] His statement: 'And he does not increase' means he does not send blessings upon the Prophet nor supplicate; due to the narration of Ahmad from Ibn Mas'ud: 'The Messenger of Allah taught me the tashahhud in the first and last part of the prayer. When it was the middle of the prayer, he stood up after finishing the tashahhud, and when it was the last part of the prayer, he supplicated as he wished.' (2) His statement: 'only' means he does not add a surah, because the six (hadith compilers) except at-Tirmidhi narrated from Abu Qatada: 'The Messenger of Allah used to recite in the first two rak'ahs of Dhuhr and 'Asr (Surah) al-Fatiha and two surahs, and in the last two (rak'ahs) (only) al-Fatiha.' This is the sunnah as in al-Muwatta'. If he adds to al-Fatiha in them, there is no harm, and sajdah as-sahw is not obligatory according to the most correct view, as in al-Ghunyat. [3] His statement: 'it is permissible' because Muhammad narrated in al-Muwatta': 'Ibn Mas'ud used not to recite anything in the last two (rak'ahs).' And from 'Ali is also narrated the option between recitation and non-recitation in the last two (rak'ahs), as reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah. This is on the basis of sufficiency, and the sunnah is recitation in the last two (rak'ahs).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's rulings for the first sitting (middle tashahhud) and recitation in the last two rak'ahs. The matn states: 'He does not increase anything upon it in the first sitting' – meaning only tashahhud is recited, no salawat or du'a. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet taught him the tashahhud and indicated that in the middle sitting one stands up after tashahhud, while in the final sitting one may supplicate. 'And he recites in what is after the first two (rak'ahs) only al-Fatiha' – the sunnah is to recite only al-Fatiha in the third and fourth rak'ahs, based on the hadith of Abu Qatada. However, if one adds a surah or glorifies Allah, it is permissible and does not require sajdah as-sahw. 'And he sits like the first (sitting)' – i.e., he sits mutawarrikan (with the left foot spread) as in the first sitting? Actually, the matn says 'like the first' meaning the sitting posture; but the commentary does not elaborate here. The phrase 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' indicates that al-Shafi'i holds that one should sit differently in the final sitting. The author then explains the permissibility of not reciting in the last two rak'ahs, citing the practice of Ibn Mas'ud and the option from 'Ali, but states that recitation is the sunnah. Thus, the Hanafi position is that reciting al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs is sunnah, but one may also recite nothing or do tasbih, and the prayer is valid.

Key Terms
قعدة الأولى (qa'dah al-ula): The first sitting (middle of prayer) after the second rak'ah in a four-rak'ah prayer.
أخريين (ukhrayayn): The last two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer.

In the Hanafi school, reciting al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs is sunnah, but omitting it entirely is permissible and does not affect the validity of the prayer.

Not Increasing in the First Tashahhud
Rule against adding salawat or dua in first sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adding salawat or dua in the first tashahhud and explain the dalil from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
In the first tashahhud, only the tashahhud is recited; adding salawat or dua is against the sunnah.

This lesson examines the first sitting (al-qa'dah al-ūlā) in the prayer. While the second tashahhud includes salawat and supplication, the first tashahhud is restricted to the tashahhud itself. The Hanafi school bases this on the explicit practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) as narrated by Ibn Mas'ud. Understanding this distinction helps preserve the sunnah structure of the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the first sitting in a 3 or 4 rakah prayer. The matn rules that one does not add anything (salawat or dua) to the tashahhud.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud (reported by Ahmad) where the Prophet taught the tashahhud for both the beginning and end, indicating no addition in the middle.
3
Understand the implication
The hadith distinguishes between the first sitting (middle) and last sitting (end), showing that supplication is only for the last.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that sending salawat in the first tashahhud is sunnah, based on other evidences.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is to not add; doing so is contrary to the sunnah and may be makruh tanzihi.

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about the first sitting (qa'dah) in the prayer. The matn says 'he does not add upon it [the tashahhud] in the first sitting.' The commentator clarifies that this means one should not recite salawat upon the Prophet (ﷺ) nor make any supplications during the first tashahhud. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud (رضي الله عنه) reported by Ahmad, which indicates that the Prophet (ﷺ) taught the tashahhud for both the first and last sitting, and he would stand up (for the third rak'ah) after the first tashahhud without adding anything. The Hanafi school holds that in the first sitting, only the tashahhud is recited, and anything beyond that (like salawat or dua) is not part of the sunnah. The evidence is the specific practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) as narrated by Ibn Mas'ud. This ruling is found in the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Ruling on Recitation in the Last Two Rak'ahs
Permissibility of reciting only Fatiha or even silence in latter rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in the last two rak'ahs and apply the distinction between sunnah and permissible silence/tasbih.

Core Idea
In the last two rak'ahs, reciting Fatiha alone is sunnah; tasbih or silence is permissible and valid.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on what to recite in the last two rak'ahs (al-ukhrawayn) of a four-rak'ah prayer. The author states that reciting only al-Fatiha is better, but if one glorifies Allah or remains silent, it is also permissible. We will explore the textual proofs from the Sunnah and the practices of the Companions that establish this permissive yet encouraged ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Identify the specific act: recitation in the last two rak'ahs (after the first two). State the ruling: reciting Fatiha alone is sunnah muakkadah; tasbih or silence is permissible (ja'iz).
The passage explicitly says 'wa hiya afdal' (it is better) for Fatiha, and 'in sabbaha aw sakata jaza' (if he glorifies or is silent, it is permissible).
2
Locate the first dalil for Fatiha alone
Find the proof that Fatiha alone is the sunnah: the hadith of Abu Qatadah (reported by al-Tirmidhi) that the Prophet used to recite only Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr.
The commentator cites this as the sunnah, as recorded in al-Muwatta.
3
Locate the second dalil for permissibility of silence/tasbih
Identify the proof that tasbih or silence is permissible: the practice of Ibn Mas'ud (reported by Muhammad in al-Muwatta) and the narration from Ali giving choice between recitation and not reciting.
These show that not reciting Fatiha is also valid, though less reward.
4
Understand the legal implications
Recognize that adding extra recitation beyond Fatiha is not harmful and does not require sajdatay al-sahw. The permissibility of silence/tasbih is on the level of 'ijza' (sufficiency), while the sunnah is to recite Fatiha.
This distinction is key to Hanafi fiqh: sunnah act leaves reward, but omission does not invalidate.
5
Apply the ruling
Apply to a scenario: If a person in the third rak'ah of Zuhr recites only SubhanAllah instead of Fatiha, the prayer is valid but deficient in reward. The mufta bihi is to recite Fatiha.
Mufta bihi: Recite Fatiha; alternatives are valid but makruh tanzihan if done habitually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقرأ فيما بعد الأوليين الفاتحة فقط، وهي أفضل، وإن سبح، أو سكت جاز. [٢] قوله : فقط ؛ أي لا يضم السورة ، لما أخرجه السنّة إلا الترمذي عن أبي قتادة ه: «كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقرأ في الأوليين من الظهر والعصر بفاتحة الكتاب وسورتين، وفي الأخْريّين بفاتحة الكتاب»، وهذا هو السنّة كما في «الموطأ»، فإن زاد على الفاتحة فيهما فلا بأس به ولا تجب سجدتا السهو على الأصح، كما في «الغُنية». [٣] قوله : جاز ؛ لما روى محمد له في «الموطأ» : «إن ابن مسعود ه كان لا يقرأ في الأخريين شيئاً)، وعن علي له أيضاً «روي التخيير بين القراءة وعدمها في الأخريين»، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، وهذا على سبيل الإجزاء والكفاية، والمسنون هو القراءة في الأُخْرَيَين.
And he recites in what is after the first two [rak'ahs] only al-Fatiha, and it is better, and if he glorifies [tasbih] or remains silent, it is permissible. [2] His saying: 'only' means he does not add a surah, due to what was reported by al-Sunnan except al-Tirmidhi from Abu Qatadah: 'The Messenger of Allah used to recite in the first two of Zuhr and Asr the Fatiha of the Book and two surahs, and in the last two with the Fatiha of the Book.' This is the sunnah as in al-Muwatta. If he adds beyond Fatiha in them, there is no harm and sajdatay al-sahw are not obligatory according to the most correct view, as in al-Ghunyiah. [3] His saying: 'permissible' due to what Muhammad narrated for him in al-Muwatta: 'Ibn Mas'ud used not to recite anything in the last two [rak'ahs].' And from Ali also 'the choice between recitation and its absence in the last two' was narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah. This [permissibility] is on the level of sufficiency, and the sunnah is recitation in the last two.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage discusses the recitation in the last two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer. The matn states that after the first two rak'ahs, the worshipper recites only Surat al-Fatiha, and this is better. However, if he performs tasbih (glorification of Allah) or remains silent, it is also permissible. The commentator expands on two points: First, the word 'only' means that one does not join a surah to the Fatiha. The proof is the hadith of Abu Qatadah (رضي الله عنه) that the Prophet (ﷺ) recited only Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs. This is the sunnah. Adding extra recitation is not prohibited and does not require the prostration of forgetfulness. Second, the permissibility of tasbih or silence is based on the practice of Ibn Mas'ud (رضي الله عنه) who did not recite anything in the last two, and the narration from Ali (رضي الله عنه) indicating choice. The Hanafi position is that recitation of Fatiha is sunnah in the last two, and it is better, but if one omits it and does tasbih or silence, the prayer is valid. The rulings here are from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
الأخريين (al-ukhrawayn): The last two rak'ahs of a four-rak'ah prayer (e.g., Zuhr, Asr, Isha).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid
B) It is permissible but less rewarded
C) He must perform sajdah al-sahw
D) He must repeat the prayer

Key Takeaway
In the last two rak'ahs, reciting Fatiha is sunnah muakkadah; tasbih or silence is permissible, and the prayer remains valid.

Hanafi Position on Sitting Posture in Final Tashahhud
Hanafi view that final sitting is like first, opposing Shafi'i tawarruk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on sitting posture in the final tashahhud, explain the key difference with the Shafi'i school, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The Hanafi school holds that men sit in iftirāsh in both sittings; tawarruk is for women only.

This lesson examines a concise but important ruling from the Hanafi matn regarding how a man should sit during the final tashahhud. The text directly states the Hanafi view and flags the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding this ikhtilaf helps you appreciate how different schools derive rulings from the same sunnah and how that affects daily prayer practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the sitting (qa'dah) in the salah — specifically the final tashahhud in prayers of three or four rak'ahs. The ruling concerns the recommended posture, not the validity of the prayer.
The matn assumes knowledge of the first sitting posture.
2
State the Hanafi position
The matn says 'he sits like the first [sitting]' which is iftirāsh: sitting on the left foot with the right foot upright. This is the sunnah for men.
3
Note the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the final sitting should be tawarruk: sitting on the left buttock with both feet protruding to the right. This is a well-known ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the proof basis
The Hanafi school relies on the general practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) without differentiation between sittings. The Shafi'i school relies on specific hadiths showing tawarruk.
The matn does not detail proofs, but the ikhtilaf is rooted in different interpretations of hadith.
5
Recognize the practical difference
In practice, a Hanafi man sits on his left foot with right foot upright; a Shafi'i man sits on his left buttock. Knowing this helps avoid confusion in congregational prayer and respects madhhab differences.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقعد كالأولى خلافاً للشافعي
And he sits like the first [sitting], contrary to al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This is a brief statement of a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The matn says that in the final tashahhud (the second sitting in a prayer of three or four rak'ahs), the worshipper sits in the same posture as the first sitting (which is iftirāsh: sitting on the left foot with the right foot upright). The Shafi'i school, on the other hand, holds that the final sitting should be in the tawarruk posture (sitting on the left buttock with both feet out to the right). The Hanafi evidence is based on general practice of the Prophet (ﷺ) who did not differentiate between the sittings in posture. The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school for men is iftirāsh in both sittings, while for women tawarruk is preferred (as will be discussed in the next lesson). This statement appears in the matn as a concise ruling.

Key Terms
التورك (al-tawarruk): Sitting on the left buttock with both feet protruding to the right side.
الافتراش (al-iftirāsh): Sitting on the left foot with the right foot upright.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the Hanafi ruling on sitting posture in the final tashahhud for men?
A) Tawarruk
B) Like the first sitting (iftirāsh)
C) Squatting
D) Standing

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds iftirāsh for men in the final tashahhud, contrary to the Shafi'i school which holds tawarruk.

Women's Sitting Posture in Tashahhud
Definition of women's tawarruk with three prophetic postures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women's sitting posture in tashahhud and explain why it is sunnah.

Core Idea
For women, tawarruk (sitting on left buttock with legs to the right) is the sunnah because it is more modest.

In this lesson, we examine the specific sitting posture prescribed for women during the tashahhud in prayer. The Hanafi school distinguishes the women's posture from men's based on the principle of modesty. The passage defines tawarruk and cites three prophetic traditions, selecting the first as the sunnah for women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage gives a ruling on how a woman sits during tashahhud. The matn states 'the woman sits on her left buttock, extending her legs from the right side.' The ruling is sunnah (recommended).
The plural 'sittings' refers to both the first and second tashahhud.
2
Define the key term 'tawarruk'
The commentator explains 'tawarruk' as sitting on the hip (al-wark). This is the act of resting on the left buttock with legs out to the right.
Tawarruk is not exclusive to women; the Prophet ﷺ did it in the final sitting, but Hanafis prescribe it specifically for women.
3
Recognize the three transmitted postures
The commentator lists three postures from the Prophet ﷺ: (1) placing hip on ground, feet out right; (2) iftirāsh (left foot spread, right foot erect); (3) left foot between thigh and shin, right foot spread. The first is for Hanafi women.
Posture 2 is for Shafi'i men in final tashahhud; posture 3 is another variant.
4
Understand the Hanafi preference
The Hanafi school selects posture 1 for women because it is more concealing (astar) and more suitable (aliq) for them. This is the mufta bihi (relied upon) position.
The ruling is sunnah muakkadah; if a woman sits like a man, her prayer is valid but disliked (makruh).
5
Note the cross-madhhab comparison
The Shafi'i school also prescribes tawarruk for women but sometimes with different details (e.g., sitting on left buttock while keeping feet on the right side differently).
Both schools agree on modesty as the basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرأة تجلس على إليتها اليُسرى مُخْرجةً رجليها من الجانب الأيمن فيهما. [١] قوله : التورك ؛ هوا الجلوس على الورك : بكسر الراء هو ما فوق الفخذ، وله كيفيات ثلاثة نقلت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في القعدة الأخيرة : أحدها: أن يفضي بوركِهِ إلى الأرض ويخرج بقدميه من الجانب الأيمن، أخرجه أبو داود، وهذا هو المسنون عندنا للنساء ؛ لأنّ فيه سترا وهو أليق بحالهن. وثانيها : أن يفرش اليُسرى ويقعد على الأرض وينصب اليمني ، أخرجه البخاري وهذا هو المسنون عند أكثر الشافعية للرجال في الأخيرة. وثالثها : أن يجعل قدمه اليسرى بين فخذه وساقه، ويفرش اليمنى(۱)، أخرجه مسلم.
And the woman sits on her left buttock, extending her legs from the right side in both [sittings]. [1] His saying: 'tawarruk' is sitting on the hip (al-wark, with kasr of the ra: what is above the thigh). There are three postures transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in the final sitting: one: he places his hip on the ground and brings out his feet from the right side, reported by Abu Dawud. That is the sunnah according to us for women, because it involves concealment and is more suitable for them. Second: he spreads the left [foot] and sits on the ground and erects the right [foot], reported by Bukhari. That is the sunnah according to most Shafi'is for men in the final [sitting]. Third: he places his left foot between his thigh and shin, and spreads the right [foot], reported by Muslim.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage provides the specific ruling for women's sitting posture in prayer. The matn states that a woman sits on her left buttock while extending both legs out to the right side, and this applies to both the first and second tashahhuds. The commentator, expanding on the concept of tawarruk, describes three sitting postures transmitted from the Prophet (ﷺ) for the final sitting. The first is: placing the hip on the ground with both feet out to the right. This is the sunnah for women according to the Hanafi school because it is more modest and concealing. The second is: sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect (iftirāsh), which is the Shafi'i sunnah for men. The third is: placing the left foot between thigh and shin with the right foot spread, which is also a variation. The Hanafi school specifically chooses the first posture for women based on the principle of modesty (satr). This ruling is found in the Fatawa literature and is relied upon.

Key Terms
الورك (al-wark): The hip; the area above the thigh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Iftirāsh like men
B) On left buttock with legs extended to the right
C) Squatting
D) Standing

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sunnah for women in tashahhud is tawarruk: sitting on the left buttock with legs to the right, for modesty.

Sending Salawat in Tashahhud: Ruling and Preferred Wording
Recommended wording for salawat in the final tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sending salawat in the final tashahhud and identify the preferred wording according to the Hanafi imams.

Core Idea
Sending salawat upon the Prophet in the final tashahhud is sunnah muakkadah; the Ibrahimic formula is the preferred choice.

This lesson addresses the act of sending blessings (salawat) upon the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) during the final tashahhud of the prayer. While any wording is permissible, the Hanafi school emphasizes using the transmitted formulas, particularly the one that parallels the blessings upon Ibrahim. The passage clarifies the legal status and the recommended practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sending salawat upon the Prophet in the final tashahhud. Determine its hukm level (fard, wajib, sunnah, etc.) from the text.
The text classifies it as sunnah muakkadah.
2
Understand the scope of permissibility
Note that any wording is allowed, but the commentator advises restricting to the transmitted (ma'thur) formulas. Recognize the balance between flexibility and following the sunnah.
3
Memorize the preferred formula
The Hanafi imams specifically choose the Ibrahimic formula. Commit the Arabic text to memory and understand its structure: dua for blessing and dua for barakah.
اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صليت على إبراهيم إنك حميد مجيد، اللهم بارك على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما باركت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم إنك حميد مجيد
4
Apply the ruling in practice
When performing the final tashahhud, you will recite this salawat. Recognize that omitting it does not invalidate the prayer, but it is a highly recommended sunnah.
The prayer remains valid without salawat, but its virtue is diminished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصلي على النبي. [٢] قوله : ويصلّي على النبي الله ، أي بأي لفظ شاء، والأولى الاقتصار على الألفاظ المأثورة، وهي كثيرة ذكرها شمس الدين محمد بن عبد الرحمن السخاوي في رسالته: «القول البديع في الصلاة على الحبيب الشفيع، والمنقول عن أئمتنا اختيار: «اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صليت على إبراهيم إنك حميد مجيد، اللهم بارك على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما باركت على إبراهيم وعلى آل ابراهيم إنك حميد مجيد».
And he sends salawat upon the Prophet. [2] His saying: 'And he sends salawat upon the Prophet' meaning with any wording he wishes, but it is better to restrict to the transmitted wordings, which are many. Shams al-Din Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Sakhawi mentioned them in his treatise 'al-Qawl al-Badi' fi al-Salah ala al-Habib al-Shafi''. Commentary
This passage addresses the sunnah of sending salawat (blessings) upon the Prophet (ﷺ) in the final tashahhud. The matn simply states 'and he sends salawat upon the Prophet.' The commentator notes that any wording is permissible, but it is superior to use the transmitted (ma'thur) wordings. He then cites the famous Ibrahimic formula which is the preferred choice of the Hanafi imams: 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala aali Muhammad kama sallayta 'ala Ibrahim wa 'ala aali Ibrahim innaka Hamidun Majid, Allahumma barik 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala aali Muhammad kama barakta 'ala Ibrahim wa 'ala aali Ibrahim innaka Hamidun Majid.' This formula is recommended because it is comprehensive and follows the prophetic tradition. The ruling is that sending salawat is sunnah muakkadah in the final tashahhud, and it is part of the prayer's completion.

Key Terms
الصلاة على النبي (al-salah 'ala al-nabi): Sending blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) in the tashahhud.

Woman's Sitting Posture (Tawarruk) in Prayer
Explains the ruling on woman's sitting posture and definition of tawarruk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman's sitting posture in prayer and identify the three narrated forms of tawarruk with their respective schools.

Core Idea
For women, the sunnah sitting posture in prayer is tawarruk on the left hip with legs to the right in both tashahhuds.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the specific sitting posture prescribed for women in prayer. It defines tawarruk and presents three hadith-based forms of the final sitting, identifying which form is sunnah for women according to the Hanafi school. The ruling emphasizes modesty (sitr) as the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage concerns a woman's sitting posture (julus) during the tashahhud in salah. Determine the legal ruling (hukm) from the text.
The text says 'المسنون' indicating sunnah, not wajib.
2
Define tawarruk
Tawarruk is defined as sitting on the hip (wark). Understand its literal and technical meaning.
The root word is 'wark' (hip), and the posture involves resting on one hip.
3
List the three narrated forms
The passage enumerates three forms of the final sitting reported from the Prophet (peace be upon him). Identify each briefly.
Forms: (1) hip on ground, feet to right; (2) spread left foot, sit on ground, erect right; (3) left foot between thigh and shin, spread right.
4
Identify the sunnah for women
According to the Hanafi school, the first form (from Abu Dawood) is the sunnah for women. Note the reasoning given: it provides more covering and is more suitable.
The commentator states: 'وهذا هو المسنون عندنا للنساء؛ لأنّ فيه سترا وهو أليق بحالهن'.
5
Note cross-madhhab differences
Compare with the Shafi'i view: most Shafi'is consider the second form (from Bukhari) as sunnah for men in the final sitting. The third form is also narrated by Muslim.
This ikhtilaf affects which posture is emphasized in different schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرأة تجلس على إليتها اليُسرى مُخْرجةً رجليها من الجانب الأيمن فيهما فإنَّ السنة عنده في التشهد الثاني التَّورك، وهو هيئة جلوس المرأة في الصلاة، هذه: (والمرأة تجلس على إليتها اليُسرى مُخْرجة رجليها من الجانب الأيمن فيهما): أي في التشهدين. قوله: التورك؛ هوا الجلوس على الورك: بكسر الراء هو ما فوق الفخذ، وله كيفيات ثلاثة نقلت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في القعدة الأخيرة: أحدها: أن يفضي بوركِهِ إلى الأرض ويخرج بقدميه من الجانب الأيمن، أخرجه أبو داود، وهذا هو المسنون عندنا للنساء؛ لأنّ فيه سترا وهو أليق بحالهن. وثانيها: أن يفرش اليُسرى ويقعد على الأرض وينصب اليمني، أخرجه البخاري وهذا هو المسنون عند أكثر الشافعية للرجال في الأخيرة. وثالثها: أن يجعل قدمه اليسرى بين فخذه وساقه، ويفرش اليمنى، أخرجه مسلم.
And the woman sits on her left hip, extending her legs from the right side in both [tashahhuds]. For indeed, the sunnah according to him in the second tashahhud is tawarruk, and it is the posture of the woman in prayer, this: (And the woman sits on her left hip, extending her legs from the right side in both [tashahhuds]): meaning in both tashahhuds. He said: tawarruk; it is sitting on the wark (hip): with kasra of the ra, it is what is above the thigh, and it has three forms reported from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) in the final sitting: One: that he places his hip on the ground and extends his feet from the right side, reported by Abu Dawood, and this is the sunnah according to us for women because it covers and is more suitable for them. Second: that he spreads his left [foot] and sits on the ground and erects his right [foot], reported by Bukhari, and this is the sunnah according to most Shafi'is for men in the final sitting. Third: that he places his left foot between his thigh and shin, and spreads his right foot, reported by Muslim.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the sitting posture of women in prayer. It first states the ruling: a woman sits on her left hip (iliya) and extends her legs out from the right side, and this is done in both tashahhuds (first and second). The commentator then defines 'tawarruk' as sitting on the hip (wark). He then presents three narrated forms of the final sitting, noting that for women, the sunnah is the first form (placing the hip on the ground and extending feet to the right) because it provides more covering and modesty. The second form is sunnah for men according to most Shafi'is, and the third form is also narrated. The Hanafi position is that women adopt the first form in both tashahhuds, while men sit differently (ifrash in the first, tawarruk in the second). The proofs are hadith from Abu Dawood, Bukhari, and Muslim.

Key Terms
التورك (al-tawarruk): Sitting on the hip (wark) with legs positioned to one side; for women, the sunnah is to sit on the left hip with legs out to the right.
الإلية (al-iliya): The hip or buttock; specifically the left hip in this context.
الورك (al-wark): The area above the thigh, the hip joint.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The sunnah for women in both tashahhuds is tawarruk: sitting on the left hip with legs extended to the right, because it provides greater modesty.

The Final Sitting in Prayer: Tashahhud, Salat, Dua, and Taslim
Detailed explanation of sunnah acts in the final tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah actions of the final sitting in Hanafi prayer and identify whether each is wajib or sunnah, supported by the textual proofs.

Core Idea
Taslim is wajib; salat and dua are sunnah; dua must avoid worldly speech.

The final sitting of the prayer includes several important acts after the tashahhud. This lesson unpacks the rulings and recommended practices for sending blessings on the Prophet, making supplication, and concluding with taslim. The source text provides both the sequence and the evidence from the Sunnah, helping distinguish between obligatory and recommended elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence
Read the first sentence of the passage to list the four actions in order: tashahhud, salat upon the Prophet, dua, and taslim.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For each action, note whether the text and commentary classify it as wajib or sunnah. The taslim is explicitly linked to the Prophet's established practice, while salat and dua are described with recommended wordings.
In Hanafi fiqh, tashahhud and taslim are wajib; salat and dua are sunnah.
3
Examine the evidence for dua
The passage cites the hadith 'then let him choose whatever supplication pleases him' (Bukhari) and the prohibition of worldly speech (Abu Dawood). Understand how these together define the scope of permissible dua.
Dua must resemble Quranic or transmitted supplications, not everyday conversation.
4
Note the preferred wording for salat
The commentator mentions the preferred formula transmitted from the imams: 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad...' as recorded by al-Sakhawi.
One may use any wording, but the transmitted formula is better.
5
Understand the intention in taslim
The passage explains that one intends by the salam to greet the angels and fellow worshippers on the right and left.
The sunnah wording is 'Al-salamu 'alaykum wa rahmatullah' with optional 'wa barakatuh'.

[2] قوله : ويصلّي على النبي الله، أي بأي لفظ شاء، والأولى الاقتصار على الألفاظ المأثورة، وهي كثيرة ذكرها شمس الدين محمد بن عبد الرحمن السخاوي في رسالته: «القول البديع في الصلاة على الحبيب الشفيع، والمنقول عن أئمتنا اختيار: «اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صليت على إبراهيم إنك حميد مجيد، اللهم بارك على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما باركت على إبراهيم وعلى آل ابراهيم إنك حميد مجيد». (٤) قوله : لا كلام; أي لا يدعو بما يشبه كلام الناس; فإنّه روى أبو داود عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إنّ هذه الصلاة لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس» الحديث، فينبغي الاحتراز عما يشبهه. [٥] قوله : ثم يسلّم ; أي يقول : السلام عليكم ورحمة الله، هذا هو المسنون، وورد في رواية لأبي داود زيادة : وبركاته أيضاً. [٦] قوله : من ثمة ; بفتح الثاء المثلثة، وتشديد الميم، بمعنى : هناك; أي ينوي بخطاب السلام الذي عن يمينه من الناس المصلين معه، والملائكة الكرام الكاتبين والحفظة. [٧] قوله : ثم عن يساره كذلك ; أي يسلم عن يساره بنية من هناك، هذا هو الثابت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بروايات كثيرة.
And he recites the tashahhud and sends blessings upon the Prophet, and he makes supplication with what resembles the Quran, and the transmitted supplication, not the speech of people. Then he says salam to his right with the intention of those present from angels and humans, then to his left similarly. [2] He said: And he sends blessings upon the Prophet, meaning with any wording he wishes, but it is better to restrict to transmitted wordings, which are many, mentioned by Shams al-Din Muhammad ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Sakhawi in his treatise: 'Al-Qawl al-Badi' fi al-Salat 'ala al-Habib al-Shafi', and what is transmitted from our imams is the choice: 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala ali Muhammad kama sallayta 'ala Ibrahim innaka Hamid Majid, Allahumma barik 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala ali Muhammad kama barakta 'ala Ibrahim wa 'ala ali Ibrahim innaka Hamid Majid.' [1] He said: And he makes supplication, i.e., after finishing the salat upon the Messenger of Allah, the hadith: 'Then let him choose after that whatever speech he wishes,' and in another narration: 'Then let him choose whichever supplication pleases him most,' reported by Bukhari and others. [2] He said: 'With what resembles the Quran,' meaning the supplications mentioned in the Quran, and those that convey similar meanings and benefits. [3] He said: 'And the transmitted' is connected to 'with what resembles'; i.e., he makes supplication with what is transmitted from the Prophet and his companions, or to 'the Quran'; i.e., he makes supplication with what resembles the transmitted. (4) He said: 'Not the speech of people'; i.e., he should not make supplication with what resembles the speech of people; for Abu Dawood narrated from the Prophet: 'This prayer is not suitable for any speech of people,' so one should avoid what resembles that. [5] He said: 'Then he says salam,' i.e., he says: 'Al-salamu 'alaykum wa rahmatullah,' this is the sunnah, and in a narration from Abu Dawood there is an addition: 'wa barakatuh.' [6] He said: 'From there' (min thamma): with fatha on the tha, and tashdid on the mim, meaning 'there'; i.e., he intends by the address of salam to those to his right from the people praying with him and the noble angels who record and protect. [7] He said: 'Then to his left similarly,' i.e., he says salam to his left with the intention of those there, this is established from the Messenger of Allah through many narrations.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the final sitting in prayer. It describes the sunnah sequence after the tashahhud: sending blessings upon the Prophet, making supplication using Quranic or transmitted duas (not resembling ordinary speech), and then saying salam to the right and left. The commentator elaborates: the salat upon the Prophet can be in any wording, but the preferred is the transmitted formula by al-Sakhawi. The supplication should be from the Quran or transmitted from the Prophet and companions, and one must avoid speech resembling worldly conversation, supported by the hadith 'This prayer is not suitable for any speech of people.' The salam is said with the intention of greeting the angels and fellow worshippers. The sunnah wording is 'Al-salamu 'alaykum wa rahmatullah' with an optional 'wa barakatuh'. This is established by multiple hadith.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of the testimony of faith in prayer: 'At-tahiyyatu lillahi...'
الصلاة على النبي (al-salat 'ala al-nabi): Sending blessings upon the Prophet, e.g., 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad...'
التسليم (al-taslim): Saying 'Al-salamu 'alaykum wa rahmatullah' to the right and left to conclude the prayer.

C) It is wajib.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
The final sitting combines wajib (taslim) and sunnah (salat, dua) acts; dua must be from Quranic or transmitted supplications, avoiding worldly speech.

Du'a in Salah: Resembling Quranic or Transmitted Supplications
Ruling on du'a content after tashahhud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three guidelines for du'a after tashahhud and apply the prohibition against resembling human speech.

Core Idea
Du'a in salah must be from the Quran or transmitted supplications, never human conversation.

After tashahhud and sending blessings on the Prophet, the worshipper may make du'a. But what kind of du'a? The Hanafi school teaches that it must resemble the Quran or the transmitted supplications (al-ma'thur), and it must not resemble ordinary human speech. This lesson unpacks the proof and the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly does the text rule upon? The act is making du'a after tashahhud and salawat. The ruling is that this du'a is recommended (mustahabb), not obligatory. It has conditions: it must be in the form of Quranic or transmitted supplications and avoid resembling human speech.
The hadith 'then let him choose' indicates choice, not compulsion.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadiths are cited: one from Bukhari and others (yukhayyar) and one from Abu Dawud (no human speech in prayer). The first establishes optionality, the second establishes the restriction.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'يختار' (choose) proves that the du'a is optional (mustahabb). The prohibition of resembling human speech is derived from the explicit forbiddance of human speech in prayer, which by extension includes any du'a that sounds like human conversation.
The hadith does not forbid all du'a, only that which resembles kalam an-nas.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school generally allows any du'a in Arabic, even if not from Quran or transmitted sources, as long as it is in the form of supplication. The Hanafi school narrows it to avoid resemblance to worldly talk.
The practical difference: Hanafi would discourage a du'a like 'O Allah, give me a car' if phrased colloquially, while Shafi'i might allow it.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that a Hanafi must ensure their du'a after tashahhud is free from wording that could be used in everyday conversation, even if in Arabic. This affects how one memorizes and recites du'as in prayer.
For example, saying 'Allahumma irhamni' is fine, but 'Ya Rabb, I need money' would be problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتشهد ويصلي على النبي الله، ويدعو بما يُشبه القرآن، والمأثور من الدُّعاء لا كلامَ النَّاسِ.
قوله : ويدعو ؛ أي بعد الفراغ من الصلاة على رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم الحديث : «ثم ليتخيّر بعد من الكلام ما شاء» ) ، وفي رواية : « ثمّ ليتخيّر من الدعاء أعجبه إليه (۲) ، أخرجه البخاري وغيره.
(٢) قوله : بما يشبه القرآن؛ أي بالأدعية المذكورة في القرآن، وبالتي تؤدّي مؤداها وتفيد مفادها.
[٣] قوله : والمأثور ؛ عطف على «بما يشبه» ؛ أي يدعو بالمنقول عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه الله ، أو على القرآن ؛ أي يدعو بما يشبه المنقول.
(٤) قوله : لا كلام ؛ أي لا يدعو بما يشبه كلام الناس ؛ فإنّه روى أبو داود عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إنّ هذه الصلاة لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس»(۳) الحديث، فينبغي الاحتراز عما يشبهه.
And he recites the tashahhud and sends blessings upon the Prophet, and supplicates with what resembles the Quran and the transmitted supplications, not the speech of people. 
His statement: 'and supplicates' — i.e., after finishing the salat upon the Messenger of Allah, the hadith: 'Then let him choose after that from speech whatever he wishes,' and in another narration: 'Then let him choose from supplication what is most pleasing to him,' narrated by Bukhari and others. 
(2) His statement: 'with what resembles the Quran' — i.e., with the supplications mentioned in the Quran, and those which convey their meaning and produce their effect. 
[3] His statement: 'and the transmitted' — it is a conjunction to 'with what resembles'; i.e., he supplicates with what is transmitted from the Prophet and his companions, or it is a conjunction to 'the Quran'; i.e., he supplicates with what resembles the transmitted. 
(4) His statement: 'not the speech' — i.e., he does not supplicate with what resembles the speech of people; for Abu Dawud narrated from the Prophet: 'Indeed in this prayer, nothing of the speech of people is suitable,' the hadith, so one should avoid what resembles it.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the permissible content of du'a after the tashahhud and salawat in salah. The matn states three principles: the du'a should resemble Quranic wording, be from transmitted supplications, and avoid resembling human speech. The commentary expands each. The hadith 'then let him choose from speech whatever he wishes' indicates that the du'a is optional after the obligatory elements. The phrase 'resembles the Quran' means using the exact supplications found in the Quran or those that convey their meaning. 'Transmitted' refers to supplications from the Prophet or companions. The prohibition of resembling human speech is based on the hadith that prayer is not suitable for human conversation, so one must avoid requests like those made between people. The commentary emphasizes that even if the wording is not exactly human speech, it should not resemble it in content. This is a Hanafi ruling; the proof is from authentic hadith.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of the testimony of faith (ashhadu an la ilaha illa Allah...) in the sitting posture of prayer.
المأثور (al-ma'thur): The supplications transmitted from the Prophet and his companions.
كلام الناس (kalam al-nas): Human conversational speech used in daily interactions, which is prohibited in salah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible as long as it is in Arabic.
B) It is not allowed because it may resemble human speech.
C) It is obligatory to use only Quranic du'as.
D) It is prohibited only if it is in a non-Arabic language.

Key Takeaway
Make du'a after tashahhud with Quranic or transmitted supplications, avoiding any wording that could be mistaken for everyday conversation.

Intention in the Taslim: Rulings for Imam, Follower, and One Praying Alone
Intra-Hanafi dispute on niyyah in taslim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whom the imam, follower, and munfarid intend in the taslim, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the imam's intention.

Core Idea
The taslim is a greeting requiring intention; the imam intends both sides, the follower intends the imam on his side, and the one alone intends the angels.

The taslim that concludes the prayer is not a mere exit; it is a greeting directed to those present and the angels. The Hanafi school emphasizes that intention (niyyah) is required to specify the addressees. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details the rulings for the imam, the follower (ma'mum), and one praying alone (munfarid), along with several intra-Hanafi disagreements on the scope of intention.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling from the matn
Identify the default rule for the imam: he intends both angels and congregation with each salam. For the follower: he intends the imam on the side where the imam is, and in both sides if opposite (muḥādhi). For the munfarid: he intends only the angels.
The matn is the base text being commented upon.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi disputes
The commentary introduces three variants: (1) some say the imam does not intend at all (gesture suffices), (2) Abu Yusuf says the imam intends only the first salam, (3) Muhammad and a narration from Abu Hanifa say the imam intends both salams. Also, the follower’s intention when opposite is specified as both (Muhammad) vs. only first (Abu Yusuf).
These are all within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi is determined later.
3
Analyze the proofs and rationale
The dispute turns on whether intention is necessary when the imam’s gesture is visible. One side argues that gesture is sufficient; the other holds that intention is a fundamental element of worship. The commentator does not cite a specific hadith but relies on general principles.
The argument_type is dalil_naqli; the practice is established by many narrations.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (as verified by al-Hidayah and al-Jami' al-Saghir) is that the imam intends with both salams, and the follower intends the imam on his side or in both if opposite. The weaker opinion (Abu al-Yusr) is not operative for fatwa.
Tarjih authority: al-Marghinani and al-Sadr al-Shahid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ يسلم عن يمينه بنيَّةٍ مَن ثَمَّة من الملك والبشر، ثم عن يساره كذلك.
قوله : ثم يسلّم ؛ أي يقول : السلام عليكم ورحمة الله ، هذا . وورد في رواية لأبي داود زيادة : وبركاته أيضاً. هو المسنون ،
[٦] قوله : من ثمة ؛ بفتح الثاء المثلثة، وتشديد الميم، بمعنى : هناك ؛ أي ينوي بخطاب السلام الذي عن يمينه من الناس المصلين معه، والملائكة الكرام الكاتبين والحفظة.
[٧] قوله : ثم عن يساره كذلك ؛ أي يسلم عن يساره بنية من هناك، هذا هو الثابت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بروايات كثيرة.
والمؤتم ينوي إمامه في جانبه ، وفيهما إن حاذاه ، والإمام بهما، والمنفرد الملك فقط والمؤتم ينوي إمامه في جانبه ، وفيهما إن حاذاه (۲)، والإمام بهما) : أي ينوي الإمام بالتسليمتين (۳). وعند البعض الإمامُ لا ينوي ؛ لأنه يشير إلى القوم ، والإشارة فوق النيَّة). وعند البعض الإمام ينوي بالتسليمة الأولى. [٢] قوله : والمنفرد ؛ أي ينوي الذي ليس بإمام ولا مقتد بسلامه الملائكة فقط ، فإنه ليس معه أحد من البشر.
(۱) يعني إذا كان الإمام في الجانب الأيمن ينويه ، وكذا إن كان في يساره . ينظر: «شرح الوقاية لابن ملك (ق۱۹/ب).
(۲) لأن المحاذي ذو حظ . . من الجانبين، وهو قول محمد ورواية عن أبي حنيفة ، واقتصر أبو يوسف على نيته في التسليمة الأولى فقط . ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(۱ : ۲۷۰).

(٥) يعني ينوي بسلامه الحفظة فقط ؛ لأنه ليس معه أحد من البشر سواهم. ينظر: «الهداية» (۱ : .(٥٣
Then he says the salam towards his right with the intention of those who are there, from the angels and people, then towards his left similarly.
His statement: 'then he says salam' — i.e., he says: 'al-salamu alaykum wa rahmatullah', this is the sunnah. And in a narration of Abu Dawud there is the addition: 'wa barakatuh' also. It is the sunnah.
[6] His statement: 'from there' — meaning: there; i.e., he intends by the address of salam those on his right from the people praying with him and the noble recording angels and guardians.
[7] His statement: 'then towards his left similarly' — i.e., he says salam towards his left with the intention of those there; this is established from the Messenger of Allah through many narrations.
And the follower intends his imam on his side, and in both sides if he is opposite him, and the imam intends both sides, and the one praying alone intends only the angels. 
And the follower intends his imam on his side, and in both sides if he is opposite him, and the imam intends both sides: i.e., the imam intends with both salams. According to some, the imam does not intend, because he signals to the people, and signaling is above intention. And according to some, the imam intends with the first salam only. And the one praying alone intends only the angels.
(1) His statement: 'on his side' — i.e., the imam; if the imam is on the right side of the follower, he intends him in the right salam, and if on the left, then in the left.
[2] His statement: 'the one praying alone' — i.e., he who is neither imam nor follower intends by his salam only the angels, for there is no human with him.
(1) Meaning: if the imam is on the right side, he intends him, and likewise if on his left. See: Sharh al-Wiqayah li Ibn Malik (folio 19b).
(2) Because the one opposite has a share from both sides; this is the statement of Muhammad and a narration from Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf confined his intention to the first salam only. See: Fath Bab al-Inayah (1:270).
(3) Verified by the author of Al-Hidayah (1:53) and verified by al-Sadr al-Shahid in al-Jami' al-Saghir. See: al-Binayah (2:258).
(4) This is the statement of Abi al-Yusr. See: al-Binayah (2:259).
(5) Meaning: he intends by his salam only the guardian angels, because there is no human with him besides them. See: al-Hidayah (1:53).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the intention in the taslim (salam) of prayer. The matn states the basic ruling: the imam intends angels and congregation with both salams; the follower intends the imam on his side and if opposite then both salams; the one alone intends only angels. The commentary explains details and disputes. The hadith from Abu Dawud shows the sunnah wording. The core issue: the imam's intention is debated. Some say he does not need to intend because signaling suffices; others say he intends with both salams. The adopted position (mufta bihi) is that the imam intends with both salams, as per al-Hidayah. The follower's intention: if the imam is on his right, he intends him in the right salam; if on his left, in the left; if opposite (directly in front), he intends him in both. This is Muhammad's view and a narration from Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf restricts it to the first salam only. The munfarid intends only the guardian angels. The final position relied upon is that the imam intends both salams and the follower follows the side or both sides if opposite.

Key Terms
التسليم (al-taslim): The act of saying 'al-salamu alaykum' to conclude the prayer.
النية (al-niyyah): The intention with which the taslim is performed, determining whom the salam is addressed to.
المؤتم (al-mu'tam): The follower in congregational prayer.
المنفرد (al-munfarid): One who prays alone, not as imam or follower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the imam is standing directly in front of the follower (mu’tam), how should the follower intend in the salams?
A) Intend the imam only in the right salam.
B) Intend the angels only in both salams.
C) Intend the imam in both salams.
D) Intend the imam in the left salam only.

Key Takeaway
The taslim requires specific intention: imam intends both sides, follower intends imam on his side or both if opposite, munfarid intends angels.

Intention for Salam: Imam, Follower, and Individual
Ruling on intention for salam for imam, muqtadi, and munfarid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on intention for salam for each prayer role and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the imam's intention.

Core Idea
The imam must intend both salams; gesture does not replace intention.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details the specific intentions required for the concluding salams in prayer. For the imam, there is a notable intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars argue that his gesture suffices, but the relied-upon position requires explicit intention. Understanding these nuances is essential for correct prayer practice and fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three roles
Recognize the imam, muqtadi (follower), and munfarid (individual) as distinct cases with different intention requirements.
2
State the ruling for each role
For the imam: intends both salams. For the muqtadi: intends the imam in the direction he sits (if to the right, intend right salam; if left, left; if directly behind, both). For the munfarid: intends only the guardian angels.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (Abu al-Yusr, Abu Yusuf in one view) hold that the imam does not need to intend because his gesture is sufficient or because only the first salam is for the congregation. Understand these opposing positions.
4
Understand the tarjih (preferred view)
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view, per al-Hidayah and al-Sadr al-Shahid, is that the imam must intend with both salams. Gesture does not replace intention because intention is a separate wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمؤتم ينوي إمامه في جانبه ، وفيهما إن حاذاه ، والإمام بهما، والمنفرد الملك فقط والمؤتم ينوي إمامه في جانبه ، وفيهما إن حاذاه (۲)، والإمام بهما) : أي ينوي الإمام بالتسليمتين (۳). وعند البعض الإمامُ لا ينوي ؛ لأنه يشير إلى القوم ، والإشارة فوق النيَّة). وعند البعض الإمام ينوي بالتسليمة الأولى. (والمنفرد) الملك فقط (0) ). (۱) قوله : في جانبه ؛ أي الإمام، فإن كان في الجانب الأيمن من المؤتم ينويه في التسليمة اليمنى وإن كان في الأيسر ففي اليُسرى.
And the follower intends his imam in his direction, and in both if he faces him, and the imam with both, and the individual only the angels. And the follower intends his imam in his side, and in both if he faces him, and the imam with both: meaning the imam intends with the two salams. And according to some, the imam does not intend because he gestures to the people, and gesture is above intention. And according to some, the imam intends with the first salam. And the individual only the angels. (1) His saying: 'in his side' meaning the imam; if he is on the right side of the follower, he intends him in the right salam, and if on the left, then in the left.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage states the rulings on intention (niyyah) for the concluding salams in prayer. For the imam, the relied-upon position (as per al-Hidayah and al-Sadr al-Shahid) is that he intends with both salams—meaning he makes the intention to conclude the prayer for himself and those following. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars (like Abu al-Yusr) hold that the imam does not need to intend at all because his gesture of turning to the right and left is sufficient and more expressive than an inner intention. Others (like Abu Yusuf) limit the intention to just the first salam. For the follower (muqtadi), he must intend his imam in the direction he is sitting; if the imam is to his right, he intends him in the right salam, and if to his left, in the left salam. If the follower is directly behind the imam, he intends him in both salams. For the individual praying alone (munfarid), he intends only the guardian angels (hafazah) by his salam, as no human is on his right or left.

Key Terms
المؤتم (al-mu'tam): The follower in congregational prayer who prays behind the imam.
الإمام (al-imam): The leader of congregational prayer.
المنفرد (al-munfarid): One who prays alone, not as imam or follower.
النِّيَّة (al-niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform a specific act of worship.
التسليمة (al-taslimah): The concluding salutation of prayer (as-salamu alaykum wa rahmatullah) to the right and left.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the munfarid (individual praying alone) intend with his salam?
A) The imam
B) The guardian angels only
C) Both the imam and angels
D) No intention is needed

Key Takeaway
The imam intends both salams, the follower intends the imam in his direction, and the individual intends the angels; intention is wajib and cannot be replaced by gesture.

The Obligation of Jahr for the Imam in Specific Prayers
Ruling on the obligation of jahr for the imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prayers in which the imam recites aloud, explain why the Hanafi school considers jahr wajib, and apply the ruling when a follower joins late.

Core Idea
Jahr is wajib for the imam in Jumu'ah, Eid, Fajr, and first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and 'Isha, even in qada.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi position on the imam's recitation. It clarifies that jahr is not merely recommended but obligatory, and it applies whether the prayer is on time or a make-up. Moreover, the passage addresses a practical scenario: what if a follower joins after the imam has already started reciting quietly?

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the imam reciting aloud (jahr) in specific prayers. The commentator states the phrasing 'يجهر' indicates obligation (wajib).
The Hanafi school considers the mujtahid's declaratory statement equivalent to a command from the Lawgiver.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for jahr in Fajr, Maghrib, and 'Isha is the prophetic practice narrated by Abu Dawud (mursal). For Jumu'ah and Eid, reports from Ali and Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz are cited.
The hadith is mursal, but the Hanafi school accepts mursal with corroboration.
3
Understand the scope – prayers and circumstances
Jahr is required in Jumu'ah, the two Eids, Fajr, and the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and 'Isha. Both the words 'adā' and 'qaḍā' cover on-time and make-up prayers. The ruling also applies even if there is only one follower.
The passage confirms jahr in all these prayers, not restricted to a congregation size.
4
Analyze the scenario of a latecomer
If a person starts a loud prayer alone reciting quietly, and a follower joins after he has read some or all of al-Fātiha, the imam must recite al-Fātiha again aloud (according to al-Khulāṣa) or at least recite the remainder aloud (according to al-Qunyah).
This shows the ruling of jahr is not waived even when the imam initially intended to pray alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القراءة ( يجهر الإمام في الجمعة والعيدين والفجر ، وأُولى العشائين أداء وقضاء ) قوله : يجهر ؛ هذا مشعر بوجوب الجهر ؛ فإنّ الإخبار من المجتهد كالإخبار من الشارع في كونه في حكم الأمر كذا في «الكافي». قوله : الإمام ؛ ولو كان المقتدى واحداً ، فلو كان يصلّي وحده الجهرية ويسر، فجاء رجل واقتدى به بعد ما قرأ بعض الفاتحة أو كلّها يقرأ الفاتحة ثانياً ويجهر، ذكره في «الخلاصة»، وذكر في «القنية»: يجهر فيما بقى. قوله : في الجمعة والعيدين؛ الجهر فيهما هو المأثور عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأصحابه ، وقد قال علي الله : «من السنة الجهرُ في صلاة العيدين)، أخرجه البيهقي بسند ضعيف، وروى عبد الرزاق : أنَّ عمر بن عبد العزيز له صلّى الجمعة ركعتين وجهر فيهما». قوله : والفجر... الخ؛ هو المروي عن رسول الله أنه كان يجهر في ركعتي الفجر، وأولي المغرب وأولي العشاء، ويسرّ في ركعاتهما الباقية، وفي الظهر والعصر أخرجه أبو داود في «مراسيله»، وفي الباب أخبار بسطناها في «السعاية» قوله : أداءً وقضاء ؛ حال أو تمييز، والمصدر بمعنى اسم الفاعل إن كان الموصوف هو الإمام، وبمعنى اسم المفعول إن كان الموصوف الصلاة، وهو متعلّق بكلّ
Section on recitation. The imam recites aloud in Jumu'ah, the two Eids, Fajr, and the first two rak'ahs of the two evening prayers (Maghrib and 'Isha), whether performed in time (ada) or make-up (qada). His saying: 'recites aloud' – this indicates the obligation of raising the voice, for the statement of the mujtahid is like the statement of the Lawgiver in being in the position of a command, as in al-Kafi. His saying: 'the imam' – even if there is only one follower. If he was praying the loud prayer alone and reciting quietly, and a man came and followed him after he had recited part or all of al-Fatiha, he recites al-Fatiha again and recites aloud, as mentioned in al-Khulasah. And it is mentioned in al-Qunyah: he recites aloud in what remains. His saying: 'in Jumu'ah and the two Eids' – reciting aloud in them is reported from the Messenger of Allah and his Companions. Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'It is from the sunnah to recite aloud in the two Eid prayers,' narrated by al-Bayhaqi with a weak chain. And Abd al-Razzaq narrated that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz led the Jumu'ah prayer with two rak'ahs and recited aloud in them. His saying: 'and Fajr... etc.' – it is reported from the Messenger of Allah that he used to recite aloud in the two rak'ahs of Fajr, the first two of Maghrib, and the first two of 'Isha, and recite quietly in the remaining rak'ahs, and in Zuhr and Asr; this was narrated by Abu Dawud in his Mursal. And there are other reports on this topic which we have elaborated in al-Si'ayah. His saying: 'ada and qada' – it is a hal or tamyiz, and the masdar is in the sense of the active participle if the described is the imam, and in the sense of the passive participle if the described is the prayer, and it is connected to each.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage establishes the ruling that the imam must recite aloud (jahr) in certain prayers: Jumu'ah, the two Eid prayers, Fajr prayer, and the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and 'Isha. This applies whether the prayer is performed on time (ada) or as a make-up (qada). The commentator explains that the phrasing 'the imam recites aloud' indicates obligation (wujub), because a mujtahid's statement about the ruling is like the Lawgiver's statement in terms of conveying a command. Even if there is only one follower, the imam must recite aloud. If a person was praying the loud prayer alone and reciting quietly, then a follower joins him after he has read some or all of al-Fatiha, he must repeat al-Fatiha aloud (according to al-Khulasah) or at least recite aloud the remainder (according to al-Qunyah). The proof for jahr in Jumu'ah and Eid is the reported practice of the Prophet and the Companions, including a statement of Ali and the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, though the chain is weak. For Fajr, Maghrib, and 'Isha, the proof is the prophetic practice narrated by Abu Dawud in his Mursal collection. The term 'ada and qada' is clarified: it refers to the prayer being performed in time or as a make-up, and the ruling of jahr applies in both cases.

Key Terms
الجهر (al-jahr): Reciting aloud so that others can hear.
أداء (ada'): Performing a prayer within its prescribed time.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed prayer after its time has expired.
العشائين (al-isha'ayn): The two evening prayers: Maghrib and 'Isha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The second two rak'ahs of Maghrib
C) Fajr and the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and 'Isha
D) Only Jumu'ah and Eid

Key Takeaway
Jahr is wajib for the imam in Jumu'ah, Eid, Fajr, and the first two rak'ahs of Maghrib and 'Isha, both in ada and qada, and even with a single follower.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
